As for simulation security, we say that FE satisfies subexponential security if  $negl(\lambda) = 2^{-\lambda^{\Omega(1)}}$ .

We also define secret-key function hiding FE as follows.

**Definition 4.5** (Function Hiding Indistinguishability security). We say a secret-key FE scheme FE for functionality  $\mathcal{F} = \{\mathcal{F}_{\lambda,\mathsf{prmtr}}\}_{\lambda,\mathsf{prmtr}}$  is IND-FH secure if for all stateful PPT adversaries  $\mathcal{A}$ , and all polynomial paramters  $\mathsf{prmtr} = (n,d,\ell,\mathsf{size})$  there exists a negligible function negl such that, we have:

$$\mathsf{adv}^{\mathsf{IND-FH}}_{\mathsf{FE},\mathcal{A}}(\lambda) := 2 \cdot |1/2 - \Pr[1 \leftarrow \mathsf{IND} - \mathsf{FH}^{\mathsf{FE}}_{\mathcal{A}}(1^{\lambda},\mathsf{prmtr})]| < \mathsf{negl}(\lambda),$$

where the experiment  $\mathsf{IND}-\mathsf{FH}^{\mathsf{FE}}_{\mathcal{A}}(1^{\lambda},\mathsf{prmtr})$  is defined below.

```
\begin{split} &\frac{\mathsf{IND}-\mathsf{FH}^{\mathsf{FE}}_{\mathcal{A}}(1^{\lambda},\mathsf{prmtr}):}{\{x_0^i,x_1^i\}_{i\in[Q_{\mathsf{ct}}]},\{f_0^j,f_1^j\}_{j\in[Q_{\mathsf{sk}}]}\leftarrow\mathcal{A}_1(1^{\lambda})}\\ &\mathsf{pp}\leftarrow\mathsf{PPGen}(1^{\lambda},\mathsf{prmtr})\\ &\mathsf{msk}\leftarrow\mathsf{Setup}(\mathsf{pp}),b\leftarrow_{_{\!R}}\{0,1\}\\ &\forall i\in[Q_{\mathsf{ct}}]:\mathsf{ct}_i\leftarrow\mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{msk},x_b^i),\forall j\in[Q_{\mathsf{sk}}]:\mathsf{sk}_j\leftarrow\mathsf{KeyGen}(\mathsf{msk},f_b^j)\\ &b'\leftarrow\mathcal{A}(\{\mathsf{ct}_i\}_{i\in[Q_{\mathsf{ct}}]},\{\mathsf{sk}_j\}_{j\in[Q_{\mathsf{sk}}]})\\ &\mathit{Return}\;1\;\mathit{if}\;b=b'\;\mathit{and}\;\forall\;i\in[Q_{\mathsf{ct}}],\;j\in[Q_{\mathsf{sk}}],f_0^j(x_0^i)=f_1^j(x_1^i),\;0\;\mathit{otherwise}. \end{split}
```

**Remark 4.7** (Subexponential Security). In both the definitions above, we say that the schemes satisfy subexponential security if  $negl = 2^{-\lambda^{\Omega(1)}}$ .

# <span id="page-24-0"></span>4.2 Efficiency Features

We now define various efficiency notions for PHFE (which are straightforward to adapt to FE).

{25}------------------------------------------------

<span id="page-25-1"></span>**Definition 4.6** (Linear efficiency)**.** *We say a PHFE for the functionality* F = {Fλ,prmtr}λ,prmtr *satisfies linear efficiency if there exists a polynomial* poly *such that for all security parameters* λ ∈ N *and all polynomial parameters* prmtr = (n, d, `,size)*, all messages* (x, y) ∈ Xλ,prmtr × Yλ,prmtr*, all* pp *in the support of* PPGen(1<sup>λ</sup> , prmtr)*, all* (pk, msk) *in the support of* Setup(pp) *the size of the circuit computing* Enc(pk, ·) *on the input* (x, y) *is at most* (|x| + |y|) · poly(λ)*, for some fixed polynomial* poly *where* |x| *and* |y| *denote the size of* x *and* y*, respectively.*

Now we define the notion of sublinearity for FE scheme for the functionality F (i.e. all polynomial circuits, defined in Section [3\)](#page-19-0). It was shown in a series of works [\[AJ15,](#page-68-6) [BV15,](#page-71-8) [BNPW16\]](#page-70-9) that such FE schemes for P/poly imply obfuscation (assuming subexponential security).

<span id="page-25-2"></span>**Definition 4.7** (Sublinearity)**.** *Let* FE *be an FE scheme for the functionality* F = {Fλ,prmtr}λ,prmtr*. If there exists* ∈ (0, 1) *and a polynomial* poly *such that for all tuple of polynomials* prmtr = (n, d, `,size)*, all* λ ∈ N*, all* pp *in the support of* PPGen(1<sup>λ</sup> , prmtr)*, all* (pk, msk) *in the support of* Setup(pp)*:*

- *if the size of the circuit* Enc(pk, ·) *is at most* size<sup>1</sup>− · poly(n, λ) *then* FE *is said to be sublinearly efficient. It is said to be compact if* = 1*.*
- *if for all* x ∈ {0, 1} n *, all ciphertexts* ct *in the support of* Enc(pk, x)*, the size of* ct *is at most* size<sup>1</sup>− · poly(n, λ) *then* FE *is said to be sublinearly ciphertext-efficient.*
- *if for all* x ∈ {0, 1} n *, all ciphertexts* ct *in the support of* Enc(pk, x)*, the size of* ct *is at most* ` 1− · poly(n, λ) *then* FE *is said to be sublinearly output-efficient.*

<span id="page-25-3"></span>**Remark 4.8** (levelled linear efficiency, compactness, and sublinearity)**.** More generally, we say that the scheme satisfies levelled linear efficiency or levelled compactness, or levelled sublinearity if the multiplicative factor poly(n, λ) in Definition [4.6](#page-25-1) or Definition [4.7](#page-25-2) is replaced by poly(λ, n, d), i.e. the polynomial also depends on the depth bound d.

# <span id="page-25-0"></span>**4.3 Structural Properties**

Now we define some structural properties that are very specific to our construction. First we define the notion of special structure which captures the property of a function key can be generated just by applying a linear function of the master secret key over some field along with the fact that the decryption of a ciphertext is "almost linear" (specified below).

<span id="page-25-4"></span>**Definition 4.8.** *(Special Structure\*.) We say that a functional encryption scheme* FE *for* F CIRC = {FCIRC λ,prmtr}λ,prmtr *satisfies special structure\* if there exist polynomials* h1, h2, h3, h<sup>4</sup> *such that the following holds. Recall* F CIRC λ,prmtr *for* prmtr = (n, d, `,size) *consists of all Boolean circuits with* n *bits of input,* ` *bits of output, depth* d *and size* size*.*

• *(PP Syntax.) The* pp *generated by the* PPGen(1<sup>λ</sup> , prmtr) *algorithm contains a* h1(λ)*-bit prime modulus* p*.*

{26}------------------------------------------------

- (Linear secret key Structure.) The master secret key is a vector in  $\mathbf{s} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{h_2(\lambda)}$ . For any function  $f \in \mathcal{F}_{\lambda,\mathsf{prmtr}}$ , let  $f = \{f_i\}_{i \in [\ell]}$  denote the circuit computing  $i^{th}$  bit of f. The functional secret key is of the form  $\mathsf{sk}_f = \{\mathsf{sk}_{f_i}\}_{i \in [\ell]}$  where each  $\mathsf{sk}_{f_i} = \langle \mathsf{pp}_{f_i}, \mathbf{s} \rangle + e_i \mod p$  where  $e_i \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \{0, \ldots, h_3(\lambda, n, \ell, d)\}$  and  $\mathsf{pp}_{f_i}$  is some deterministic polynomial time computable function of  $\mathsf{pp}$  and  $f_i$ .
- (Linear + Round Decryption with polynomial decryption error.) There exists a deterministic poly-time algorithm such that given an encryption ct of  $m \in \{0,1\}^n$  and a function  $f = (f_1, \ldots, f_\ell) \in \mathcal{F}_{\lambda, \mathsf{prmtr}}$ , for every  $i \in [\ell]$ , computes  $\mathsf{ct}_{f_i}$  such that  $|\mathsf{ct}_{f_i} \langle \mathsf{pp}_{f_i}, s \rangle f_i(m)\lceil \frac{p}{2}\rceil | \leq h_4(\lambda, d, \ell, \mathsf{size})$ . Given the secret-key for a function  $f = (f_1, \ldots, f_\ell)$ , this can be used to recover  $f(m) = (f_1(m), \ldots, f_\ell(m))$ .

# <span id="page-26-0"></span>5 $\epsilon$ -simulation Secure FE to Fully Secure FE

In this section, we show how to construct an IND secure FE scheme for all circuits from a  $\epsilon$ -simulation secure FE for all circuits for any  $\epsilon \in (0,1)$  (as per Definition 4.3), additionally assuming LWE. The IND secure FE (see Definition 4.4) that results from our transformation inherits the sublinear efficiency property of the underlying  $\epsilon$ -simulation secure FE.

# <span id="page-26-1"></span>5.1 Homomorphic Secret Sharing

We first recall the notion of a Homomorphic-Secret Sharing (HSS) scheme for circuits that will be used as one of the tools for our construction. We recall that for  $\lambda \in \mathbb{N}$ , and polynomial parameters prmtr =  $(n, d, \ell, \text{size})$ ,  $\mathcal{F}_{\lambda, \text{prmtr}}^{\text{CIRC}}$  consists of all boolean circuits with n bits of inputs,  $\ell$  bits of outputs, depth d and size size.

**Definition 5.1** (Syntax of a HSS Scheme). *An* HSS *scheme for circuits*  $\mathcal{F}^{CIRC} = \{\mathcal{F}_{\lambda,prmtr}^{CIRC}\}_{\lambda,prmtr}\}_{\lambda,prmtr}$  *consists of the following PPT algorithms:* 

- Share $(1^{\lambda}, \text{prmtr}, 1^t, x)$ : Given as input the security parameter  $1^{\lambda}$ , the tuple of polynomials  $\text{prmtr} = (n, \ell, d, \text{size})$ , a threshold  $1^t$ , and an input  $x \in \{0, 1\}^n$ , the sharing algorithm outputs t shares  $(\text{sh}_1, \ldots, \text{sh}_t)$ .
- Eval $(f, \mathsf{sh}_i)$ : Given as input a function  $f \in \mathcal{F}_{\lambda,\mathsf{prmtr}}^{\mathsf{CIRC}}$  and a share  $\mathsf{sh}_i$ , the evaluation algorithm deterministically outputs an evaluation  $\widehat{\mathsf{sh}}_i$ .
- Decode( $\{\widehat{\mathsf{sh}}_i\}_{i\in[t]}$ ): Given as input evaluations evaluations  $\widehat{\mathsf{sh}}_i$  for all  $i\in[t]$ , the decoding algorithm deterministically outputs  $z\in\{0,1\}^\ell$  or  $\bot$  if it fails.

**Correctness.** An HSS scheme is said to be correct if for all  $\lambda \in \mathbb{N}$ , all polynomials prmtr =  $(n, d, \ell, \text{size})$ ,  $t \in \mathbb{N}$ , all  $f \in \mathcal{F}_{\lambda, \text{prmtr}}^{\text{CIRC}}$ , all shares  $\text{sh}_1, \ldots, \text{sh}_t$  in the support of  $\text{Share}(1^{\lambda}, \text{prmtr}, 1^t, x)$ , all evaluations  $\widehat{\text{sh}}_1, \ldots, \widehat{\text{sh}}_t$  in the support of  $\text{Eval}(f, \text{sh}_1), \ldots, \text{Eval}(f, \text{sh}_t)$  respectively, we have  $\text{Decode}(\{\widehat{\text{sh}}_i\}_{i \in [t]}) = f(x)$ .

{27}------------------------------------------------

**Definition 5.2** (IND security). An HSS scheme for circuits HSS is said to be IND-secure if for all admissible stateful ppt adversaries A, there exists a negligible function negl, such that for all tuple of polynomials prmtr =  $(n, d, \text{size}, \ell)$  and t:

$$\mathsf{adv}^{\mathsf{Ind}}_{\mathsf{HSS},\mathcal{A}}(1^{\lambda}) := |\Pr[1 \leftarrow \mathsf{Expt}^0_{\mathcal{A}}(1^{\lambda})] - \Pr[1 \leftarrow \mathsf{Expt}^1_{\mathcal{A}}(1^{\lambda})]| < \mathsf{negl}(\lambda),$$

where the experiments  $\operatorname{Expt}_{\mathcal{A}}^b(1^{\lambda})$  for  $b \in \{0,1\}$  are defined below. We say an adversary  $\mathcal{A}$  is admissible if S is not equal to [t] and  $f(x_0) = f(x_1)$ . Further, the scheme is subexponentially secure if  $\operatorname{negl}(\lambda) = 2^{-\lambda^{\Omega(1)}}$ .

$$\frac{\mathsf{Expt}_{\mathcal{A}}^{b}(1^{\lambda}):}{(x_{1},x_{0},f)} \in (\{0,1\}^{n})^{2} \times \mathcal{F}_{\lambda,\mathsf{prmtr}} \leftarrow \mathcal{A}(1^{\lambda}) 
S \subsetneq [t] \leftarrow \mathcal{A}(1^{\lambda}) 
(\{\mathsf{sh}_{i}\}_{i\in[t]}) \leftarrow \mathsf{Share}(1^{\lambda},\mathsf{prmtr},1^{t},x_{b}) 
\forall i \in [t]: \widehat{\mathsf{sh}}_{i} \leftarrow \mathsf{Eval}(f,\mathsf{sh}_{i}) 
\alpha \leftarrow \mathcal{A}(\{\mathsf{sh}_{i}\}_{i\in S},\{\widehat{\mathsf{sh}}_{i}\}_{i\in[t]}) 
Output \alpha.$$

We now list the efficiency properties we require from the HSS scheme.

**Efficiency Properties.** There exist polynomials  $h_1, h_2, h_3, h_4, h_5$  such that for all polynomials  $n, d, \ell$ , size, t and all security parameters  $\lambda$ , the following holds:

- The circuit computing the function  $\mathsf{Share}(1^\lambda,\mathsf{prmtr},1^t,\cdot)$  is of size  $h_1(n,d,t,\lambda)$ . The length of each share is  $n\cdot h_2(\lambda,d)$
- For any function  $f \in \mathcal{F}_{\lambda,\mathsf{prmtr}}^{\mathsf{CIRC}}$ , the circuit computing the function  $\mathsf{Eval}(f,\cdot)$  is of size  $\mathsf{size} \cdot h_3(\lambda,d)$  and of depth  $d \cdot h_4(\lambda)$ .
- The output length of  $\text{Eval}(f,\cdot)$  is  $\ell \cdot h_5(\lambda,d)$

As shown in [MW16, DHRW16, BGG<sup>+</sup>18], if there exists a constant  $\rho > 0$  such that the LWE assumption with modulus-to-noise ratio  $2^{\dim^{\rho}}$  holds, then there exists an IND-secure HSS scheme for circuits.

### <span id="page-27-0"></span>5.2 Transformation

We now present our transformation.

<span id="page-27-1"></span>**Theorem 5.1.** Let  $\epsilon \in (0,1)$ , FE be a (single-key)  $\epsilon$ -simulation secure FE for  $\mathcal{F}^{CIRC}$ , and HSS be a IND-secure HSS for  $\mathcal{F}^{CIRC}$ . Then, FE<sub>amp</sub> defined below satisfies (single-key) indistinguishability security. Further, if FE and HSS are subexponentially secure, then so is FE.

Let HSS be an HSS for circuits and FE be an FE for circuits. Below is our construction  $\mathsf{FE}_{\mathsf{amp}}$  from HSS and FE.

{28}------------------------------------------------

- PPGen( $1^{\lambda}$ , prmtr): It takes as input the security parameter  $1^{\lambda}$  and the tuple of polynomials prmtr =  $(n, \ell, d, \text{size})$ . Let  $h_1, h_2, h_3, h_4, h_5$  be the polynomials associated with HSS (see the efficiency properties above). The algorithm sets the polynomials  $t(\lambda) = \lambda$ ,  $n'(\lambda) = n \cdot h_2(\lambda, d)$ ,  $\text{size}' = \text{size} \cdot h_3(\lambda, d)$ ,  $d' = d \cdot h_4(\lambda)$ ,  $\ell' = \ell \cdot h_5(\lambda, d)$  and  $\text{prmtr}' = (n', \ell', \text{size}', d')$ . Run FE.pp  $\leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}}$  FE.PPGen( $1^{\lambda}$ , prmtr'). Output pp = FE.pp.
- Setup(pp) : For all  $i \in [t]$ , run (FE.pk<sub>i</sub>, FE.msk<sub>i</sub>)  $\leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}}$  FE.Setup(pp). Output pk = (FE.pk<sub>1</sub>,..., FE.pk<sub>t</sub>) and msk = (FE.msk<sub>1</sub>,..., FE.msk<sub>t</sub>).
- Enc(pk, m): Run HSS.Share( $1^{\lambda}$ , prmtr,  $1^{t}$ , m)  $\rightarrow$  {sh<sub>i</sub>}<sub>i∈[t]</sub>. Parse pk = (FE.pk<sub>1</sub>, . . . , FE.pk<sub>t</sub>). Compute ct[i] = FE.Enc(FE.pk<sub>i</sub>, sh<sub>i</sub>) for all  $i \in [t]$ . Output ct = (ct[1], . . . , ct[t]).
- KeyGen(msk, f): Parse msk = (FE.msk<sub>1</sub>,..., FE.msk<sub>t</sub>). Let F be the function HSS.Eval(f,·). For all  $i \in [t]$ , compute  $\mathsf{sk}_F[i] \leftarrow \mathsf{FE}.\mathsf{KeyGen}(\mathsf{FE}.\mathsf{msk}_i, F)$ . Output  $\mathsf{sk}_f = (\mathsf{sk}_F[1], \ldots, \mathsf{sk}_F[t])$ .
- $\mathsf{Dec}(\mathsf{sk},\mathsf{ct}) : \mathsf{Parse} \ \mathsf{sk}_f = (\mathsf{sk}_F[1],\ldots,\mathsf{sk}_F[t]) \ \mathsf{and} \ \mathsf{ct} = (\mathsf{ct}[1],\ldots,\mathsf{ct}[t]). \ \mathsf{Compute} \ \widehat{\mathsf{sh}}_i = \mathsf{FE}.\mathsf{Dec}(\mathsf{sk}_F[i],\mathsf{ct}[i]) \ \mathsf{for} \ \mathsf{all} \ i \in [t]. \ \mathsf{Output} \ z = \mathsf{HSS}.\mathsf{Decode}(\widehat{\mathsf{sh}}_1,\ldots,\widehat{\mathsf{sh}}_t).$

**Correctness.** The correctness of the scheme follows immediately from the correctness of FE and HSS.

**Sublinear-Efficiency.** We show that the transformation preserves (levelled) sublinear (output) efficiency. Namely, we have:

- If FE satisfies (levelled) ciphertext sublinearity, then, the length of the ciphertext of FE<sub>amp</sub> is  $O(t \cdot \text{size}'^{1-\rho} \operatorname{poly}(n', d', \lambda))$  for some  $\rho > 0$ . Observe that  $t = \lambda$ ,  $\operatorname{size}' = \operatorname{size} \cdot h_3(\lambda, d)$ , and  $d' = d \cdot h_4(\lambda)$  and  $n' = n \cdot h_2(\lambda, d)$ . Thus, the size of the ciphertext is  $O(\operatorname{size}^{1-\rho} \operatorname{poly}'(n, d, \lambda))$ . Thus, the resulting scheme is also (levelled) ciphertext sublinear.
- If FE is (levelled) sublinearly efficient, then the following happens. The size of the circuit computing the encryption is  $O(h_1(n,d,t,\lambda)+t\cdot \text{size}'^{1-\rho}\operatorname{poly}(\lambda,d',n'))$ . The first part comes from the size of the circuit computing the HSS share, and the second part comes from the size of the circuit computing FE encryptions. Thus, this is  $O(\operatorname{size}^{1-\rho}\operatorname{poly}'(n,d,\lambda))$  and  $\operatorname{FE}_{\mathsf{amp}}$  is (levelled) sublinearly efficient.
- If FE satisfies (levelled) output-sublinearity, then the following happens. The size of the circuit computing the encryption is  $O(h_1(n,d,t,\lambda)+t\cdot\ell'^{1-\rho}\operatorname{poly}(\lambda,d',n'))$ . The first part comes from the size of the circuit computing the HSS share, and the second part comes from the size of the circuit computing FE encryptions. Thus, this is  $O(\ell^{1-\rho}\operatorname{poly}'(n,d,\lambda))$  and  $\operatorname{FE}_{\mathsf{amp}}$  is (levelled) output-sublinearly efficient.

**Security Proof.** We complete the proof of Theorem 5.1, namely we prove the IND security of  $FE_{amp}$  from the  $\epsilon$ -simulation security of FE and the IND security of HSS. Note that if the underlying FE and HSS are subexponentially secure, then so is  $FE_{amp}$ . We first list the hybrids and then argue indistinguishability between them inline. The first hybrid the

{29}------------------------------------------------

challenge bit is b ←<sup>R</sup> {0, 1}, whereas the last hybrid is independent of b.

#### Hybrid<sup>0</sup> :

- A outputs (m0, m1), and a function f.
- Sample b ←<sup>R</sup> {0, 1}. Compute (sh1, . . . ,sht) ←<sup>R</sup> Share(1<sup>λ</sup> , prmtr, 1 t , mb).
- Run pp ←<sup>R</sup> FE.PPGen(1<sup>λ</sup> , prmtr<sup>0</sup> ) and sample (FE.pk<sup>i</sup> , FE.mski) ←<sup>R</sup> Setup(pp) for i ∈ [t]. Set pk = (FE.pk<sup>1</sup> , . . . , FE.pk<sup>t</sup> ).
- Compute ct[i] ← FE.Enc(pk<sup>i</sup> ,shi) for i ∈ [t]. Let ct = (ct[1], . . . , ct[t]).
- Compute sk<sup>F</sup> [i] ← FE.KeyGen(FE.msk<sup>i</sup> , F) where F = HSS.Eval(f, ·) for i ∈ [t]. Let sk<sup>f</sup> = (sk<sup>F</sup> [1], . . . ,sk<sup>F</sup> [t]).
- Output (pp, pk, ct,sk<sup>f</sup> )

#### Hybrid<sup>1</sup> :

- A outputs (m0, m1), and a function f.
- Sample b ←<sup>R</sup> {0, 1}. Compute (sh1, . . . ,sht) ←<sup>R</sup> Share(1<sup>λ</sup> , prmtr, 1 t , mb).
- Run pp ←<sup>R</sup> FE.PPGen(1<sup>λ</sup> , prmtr<sup>0</sup> ) and run (FE.pk<sup>e</sup> i , FE.tdi) <sup>←</sup><sup>R</sup> Setup ^(pp) for <sup>i</sup> <sup>∈</sup> [t]. Set pk = (FE.pk<sup>e</sup> 1 , . . . , FE.pk<sup>e</sup> t ).
- Let γ<sup>i</sup> ← Sample(sh<sup>i</sup> , F).
- Compute ct[i] <sup>←</sup> FE.Enc <sup>g</sup>(FE.td<sup>i</sup> , γi) and sk<sup>F</sup> [i] <sup>←</sup> FE.KeyGen ^ (FE.td<sup>i</sup> , F, γi) for i ∈ [t].
- Let ct = (ct[1], . . . , ct[t]) and sk<sup>f</sup> = (sk<sup>F</sup> [1], . . . ,sk<sup>F</sup> [t]).
- Output (pp, pk, ct,sk<sup>f</sup> )

The hybrids above are computationally indistinguishable due to -simulation security of FE. The only difference between these hybrids is how ct[i],sk<sup>F</sup> [i] is generated for all i ∈ [t]. In Hybrid<sup>0</sup> is generated using honest Enc and KeyGen algorithms and in Hybrid<sup>1</sup> they are generated using the simulation algorithms.

#### Hybrid<sup>2</sup> :

- A outputs (m0, m1), and a function f.
- Sample b ←<sup>R</sup> {0, 1}. Compute (sh1, . . . ,sht) ←<sup>R</sup> Share(1<sup>λ</sup> , prmtr, 1 t , mb).
- Run pp ←<sup>R</sup> FE.PPGen(1<sup>λ</sup> , prmtr<sup>0</sup> ) and run (FE.pk<sup>e</sup> i , FE.tdi) <sup>←</sup><sup>R</sup> Setup ^(pp) for <sup>i</sup> <sup>∈</sup> [t]. Set pk = (FE.pk<sup>e</sup> 1 , . . . , FE.pk<sup>e</sup> t ).

{30}------------------------------------------------

- Sample α ∈ {0, 1} t . Each is independently sampled where α<sup>i</sup> = 1 with probability , and 0 otherwise. If α<sup>i</sup> = 1, set γ<sup>i</sup> = (F, F(shi)), otherwise γ<sup>i</sup> = (sh<sup>i</sup> , F).
- Compute ct[i] <sup>←</sup> FE.Enc <sup>g</sup>(FE.td<sup>i</sup> , γi) and sk<sup>F</sup> [i] <sup>←</sup> FE.KeyGen ^ (FE.td<sup>i</sup> , F, γi) for i ∈ [t].
- Let ct = (ct[1], . . . , ct[t]) and sk<sup>f</sup> = (sk<sup>F</sup> [1], . . . ,sk<sup>F</sup> [t]).
- Output (pp, pk, ct,sk<sup>f</sup> )

Hybrid<sup>1</sup> and Hybrid<sup>2</sup> are identical as we just expanded how the Sample operates. We now describe Hybrid<sup>3</sup> , where we abort when α = 0<sup>t</sup> .

#### Hybrid<sup>3</sup> :

- A outputs (m0, m1), and a function f.
- Sample b ←<sup>R</sup> {0, 1}. Compute (sh1, . . . ,sht) ←<sup>R</sup> Share(1<sup>λ</sup> , prmtr, 1 t , mb).
- Run pp ←<sup>R</sup> FE.PPGen(1<sup>λ</sup> , prmtr<sup>0</sup> ) and run (FE.pk<sup>e</sup> i , FE.tdi) <sup>←</sup><sup>R</sup> Setup ^(pp) for <sup>i</sup> <sup>∈</sup> [t]. Set pk = (FE.pk<sup>e</sup> 1 , . . . , FE.pk<sup>e</sup> t ).
- Sample α ∈ {0, 1} t . Each is independently sampled where α<sup>i</sup> = 1 with probability , and 0 otherwise. If α<sup>i</sup> = 1, set γ<sup>i</sup> = (F, F(shi)), otherwise γ<sup>i</sup> = (sh<sup>i</sup> , F). Abort if α = 0<sup>t</sup> .
- Compute ct[i] <sup>←</sup> FE.Enc <sup>g</sup>(FE.td<sup>i</sup> , γi) and sk<sup>F</sup> [i] <sup>←</sup> FE.KeyGen ^ (FE.td<sup>i</sup> , F, γi) for i ∈ [t].
- Let ct = (ct[1], . . . , ct[t]) and sk<sup>f</sup> = (sk<sup>F</sup> [1], . . . ,sk<sup>F</sup> [t]).
- Output (pp, pk, ct,sk<sup>f</sup> )

Hybrid<sup>2</sup> and Hybrid<sup>3</sup> are statistically indistinguishable with the statistical distance being Pr[α = 0<sup>t</sup> ] = (1 − ) t . If ∈ (0, 1] is a constant then this is 2 −λ Ω(1) . In the next hybrid, we share m<sup>0</sup> instead of mb.

#### Hybrid<sup>4</sup> :

- A outputs (m0, m1), and a function f.
- Compute (sh1, . . . ,sht) ←<sup>R</sup> Share(1<sup>λ</sup> , prmtr, 1 t , m0).
- Run pp ←<sup>R</sup> FE.PPGen(1<sup>λ</sup> , prmtr<sup>0</sup> ) and run (FE.pk<sup>e</sup> i , FE.tdi) <sup>←</sup><sup>R</sup> Setup ^(pp) for <sup>i</sup> <sup>∈</sup> [t]. Set pk = (FE.pk<sup>e</sup> 1 , . . . , FE.pk<sup>e</sup> t ).
- Sample α ∈ {0, 1} t . Each is independently sampled where α<sup>i</sup> = 1 with probability , and 0 otherwise. If α<sup>i</sup> = 1, set γ<sup>i</sup> = (F, F(shi)), otherwise γ<sup>i</sup> = (sh<sup>i</sup> , F). Abort if α = 0<sup>t</sup> .
- Compute ct[i] <sup>←</sup> FE.Enc <sup>g</sup>(FE.td<sup>i</sup> , γi) and sk<sup>F</sup> [i] <sup>←</sup> FE.KeyGen ^ (FE.td<sup>i</sup> , F, γi) for i ∈ [t].

{31}------------------------------------------------

- Let ct = (ct[1], . . . , ct[t]) and sk<sup>f</sup> = (sk<sup>F</sup> [1], . . . ,sk<sup>F</sup> [t]).
- Output (pp, pk, ct,sk<sup>f</sup> )

The only difference between Hybrid<sup>3</sup> and Hybrid<sup>4</sup> is that we now share m<sup>0</sup> instead of mb. We can formally show that if there is an attacker that distinguishes between these hybrids, then, it should break the security of HSS scheme. We can design the reduction as follows. Let A<sup>0</sup> be the adversary for distinguishing the hybrids. Reduction R

- A outputs (m0, m1), and a function f.
- Sample α ∈ {0, 1} t . Each α<sup>i</sup> for i ∈ [t] is sampled independently to be 1 with probability and 0 with probability 1 − . Abort if α = 0<sup>t</sup> , otherwise proceed. Let S be the set of indices where α<sup>i</sup> = 0. Note, S 6= [t].
- Sample b ←<sup>R</sup> {0, 1}. Send f, S,(mb, m0) to the HSS challenger. The reduction gets back {shi}i∈<sup>S</sup> and {F(γi)}i /∈S. If i ∈ S, set γ<sup>i</sup> = (sh<sup>i</sup> , F) otherwise γ<sup>i</sup> = (F(shi), F).
- Run pp ←<sup>R</sup> FE.PPGen(1<sup>λ</sup> , prmtr<sup>0</sup> ) and sample (FE.pk<sup>e</sup> i , FE.tdi) <sup>←</sup><sup>R</sup> Setup ^(pp) for <sup>i</sup> <sup>∈</sup> [t]. Set pk = (FE.pk<sup>e</sup> 1 , . . . , FE.pk<sup>e</sup> t ).
- Compute ct[i] <sup>←</sup> FE.Enc <sup>g</sup>(FE.td<sup>i</sup> , γi) and sk<sup>F</sup> [i] <sup>←</sup> FE.KeyGen ^ (FE.td<sup>i</sup> , F, γi) for i ∈ [t].
- Give (pp, pk, ct,sk<sup>f</sup> ) to A<sup>0</sup> . Output whatever it outputs.

Note that the view of A<sup>0</sup> is exactly as in Hybrid<sup>3</sup> if m<sup>b</sup> is shared, and it is exactly as Hybrid<sup>4</sup> if m<sup>0</sup> is shared. If A<sup>0</sup> indeed succeeds in distinguishing these two hybrids, then our Reduction will succeed in the HSS game with the same advantage.

# <span id="page-31-0"></span>**6 Definition of Structured-Seed PRG**

<span id="page-31-1"></span>**Definition 6.1** (Syntax of Structured-Seed Pseudo-Random Generators (sPRG))**.** *Let* τ *be a positive constant. A structured-seed Boolean PRG,* sPRG*, with stretch* τ *that maps* (n · poly(λ)) *bit binary strings into* (m = n τ )*-bit strings, where* poly *is a fixed polynomial, is defined by the following PPT algorithms:*

- PPGen(1<sup>λ</sup> , 1 n ) *takes as input the security parameter* λ*, and an input length* 1 n *, which is a polynomial in* λ*. It outputs public parameters* pp*, which amongst other things contains an odd prime modulus* p(λ) *which is* poly(λ) *bit prime for some polynomial independent of* n*.*
- IdSamp(pp) *samples a function index* I*.*
- SdSamp(I) *jointly samples two binary strings, a public seed and a private seed,* sd = (P, S)*. These are vectors over* Zp*. The combined dimension of these vectors is* n · poly(λ)*.*
- Eval(I,sd) *computes a string in* {0, 1} m*.*

{32}------------------------------------------------

**Remark 6.1** (The modulus  $p(\lambda)$ ). The size of the modulus  $p(\lambda)$  is some fixed polynomial in the security parameter  $\lambda$  independent of n.

**Remark 6.2** (Polynomial Stretch.). We say that an sPRG has polynomial stretch if  $\tau > 1$  for some constant  $\tau$ .

**Remark 6.3** (Linear Efficiency.). We say that an sPRG has linear-efficiency if the time to sample sd is  $n \cdot \text{poly}(\lambda)$ .

**Remark 6.4** (On  $poly(\lambda)$  multiplicative factor in the seed length.). As opposed to a standard Boolean PRG definition where the length of the output is set to be  $n^{\tau}$  where n is the seed length, we allow the length of the seed to increase multiplicatively by a fixed polynomial poly in a parameter  $\lambda$ . Looking ahead, one should view n as an arbitrary large polynomial in  $\lambda$ , and hence sPRG will be expanding in length.

**Definition 6.2** (Security of sPRG). A structured-seed Boolean PRG, sPRG, satisfies

**Pseudorandomness:** Let  $\lambda \in \mathbb{N}$  be the security parameter, let  $n(\lambda)$  be a polynomial in  $\lambda$ . Then, following distributions are indistinguishable.

$$\begin{array}{c} (\mathsf{pp}, I, \, \mathsf{P}, \, \, \mathsf{Eval}(I, \mathsf{sd})) \\ (\mathsf{pp}, I, \, \, \mathsf{P}, \, \, \boldsymbol{r} \, \, ) \end{array}$$

where  $pp \leftarrow \mathsf{PPGen}(1^{\lambda}, 1^n), \ I \leftarrow \mathsf{IdSamp}(pp), \ \mathsf{sd} \leftarrow \mathsf{SdSamp}(I), \ \boldsymbol{r} \leftarrow \{0, 1\}^m.$ 

**Definition 6.3** (Complexity and degree of sPRG). Let  $D \in \mathbb{N}$ , let  $\lambda \in \mathbb{N}$  and  $n = n(\lambda)$  be arbitrary positive polynomial in  $\lambda$ , and  $p = p(\lambda)$  denote a prime modulus which is sampled during PPGen. Let  $\mathbb{C}$  be a complexity class. A sPRG has complexity  $\mathbb{C}$  in the public seed and degree D in private seed over  $\mathbb{Z}_p$ , denoted as, sPRG  $\in (\mathbb{C}, deg D)$ , if for every I in the support of IdSamp $(1^{\lambda}, 1^n)$ , there exists an algorithm  $\operatorname{Process}_I$  in  $\mathbb{C}$  and an m(n)-tuple of polynomials  $Q_I$  that can be efficiently generated from I, such that for all sd in the support of  $\operatorname{SdSamp}(I)$ , it holds that:

$$\mathsf{Eval}(I,\mathsf{sd}) = Q_I(\mathsf{P}',\mathsf{S}) \ \textit{over} \ \mathbb{Z}_p \ , \ \mathsf{P}' = \mathsf{Process}_I(\mathsf{P}) \ ,$$

where  $Q_I$  has degree 1 in P and degree D in S.

We remark that the above definition generalizes the standard notion of families of PRGs in two aspects: 1) the seed consists of a public part and a private part, jointly sampled and arbitrarily correlated, and 2) the seed may not be uniform. Therefore, we obtain the standard notion as a special case.

**Definition 6.4** (Pseudo-Random Generators, degree, and locality). *A (uniform-seed) Boolean PRG (PRG) is an* sPRG *with a seed sampling algorithm* SdSamp(I) *that outputs a public seed* P *that is an empty string and a uniformly random private seed*  $S \leftarrow \{0,1\}^n$ , *where the polynomial* poly *is fixed to be* 1.

Let  $D, \mathsf{Loc} \in \mathbb{N}$ . The PRG has multilinear degree D if for every  $n \in \mathbb{N}$  and I in the support of  $\mathsf{IdSamp}(1^n)$ , we have that  $\mathsf{Eval}(I, \mathsf{sd})$  can be written as an m(n)-tuple of degree-D polynomials over  $\mathbb{Z}$  in  $\mathsf{S}$ . It has constant locality  $\mathsf{Loc}$  if for every  $n \in \mathbb{N}$  and I in the support of  $\mathsf{IdSamp}(1^n)$ , every output bit of  $\mathsf{Eval}(I, \mathsf{sd})$  depends on at most  $\mathsf{Loc}$  bits of  $\mathsf{S}$ .

{33}------------------------------------------------

### <span id="page-33-0"></span>6.1 Construction of sPRG and Our New Assumption

**Our New Assumption.** In this section, we describe our new assumption. Our new assumption is stronger than the one described next. The assumption is widely known in cryptography as the LWE with binary error assumption.

**Definition 6.5** (LWBE<sub> $\epsilon,\rho$ </sub> Assumption). For any constants  $\epsilon > 0$  and  $\rho > 0$ , we say that the assumption LWBE<sub> $\epsilon,\rho$ </sub> holds if for every odd prime modulus  $p = O(2^{n^{\rho}})$  the following happens. We define two distributions below. The assumption requires that the following distributions are computationally indistinguishable:

$$\frac{\mathsf{Random}_{\mathcal{A}}(1^n):}{\boldsymbol{s} \leftarrow \mathbb{Z}_p^{n^{0.5+\epsilon}}; \boldsymbol{a}_i \leftarrow \mathbb{Z}_p^{n^{0.5+\epsilon}};}\\ r_i \leftarrow \mathbb{Z}_p \forall \ i \in [n];\\ \textit{Output}\left(\left.\left\{\boldsymbol{a}_i, r_i\right\}_{i \in [n]}\right)\right.$$

Formally, we say that LWBE<sub> $\epsilon,p$ </sub> holds if for any ppt distinguisher A:

$$\mathsf{adv}^{\mathsf{LWBE}_{\epsilon,p}}_{\mathcal{A}}(1^n) := |\Pr[\mathcal{A}(z_1) = 1] - \Pr[\mathcal{A}(z_2) = 1]| < \mathsf{negl}(n),$$

where  $z_1 \leftarrow \mathsf{PseudoR}_{\mathcal{A}}(1^n)$  and  $z_2 \leftarrow \mathsf{Random}_{\mathcal{A}}(1^n)$ .

We discuss the state of this assumption in Section A.4. Next, we describe our main new assumption which can be seen as an assumption arising from the interplay between the two assumptions described above (the assumption of LWBE and that of a pseudorandom generator with large enough stretch). We discuss the plausibility of this assumption too in Section A.4.

**Definition 6.6** (G-LWEleak<sub>d,\epsilon,\rho</sub> Security). For any constant integer D>0, constants  $\epsilon>0$  and  $\rho\in(0,0.5)$ , we say that a degree D pseudorandom generator G of stretch at least  $m(n)\geq n^{\lceil\frac{D}{2}\rceil\cdot(0.5+\epsilon)+\rho}$  satisfies G-LWEleak<sub>d,\epsilon,\rho</sub>-security if for any odd prime modulus  $p=O(2^{n^{\rho}})$ , the following two distributions are computationally indistinguishable:

```
\frac{\mathsf{PseudoR}_{\mathcal{A}}^G(1^n):}{\boldsymbol{s} \leftarrow \mathbb{Z}_p^{n^{0.5+\epsilon}}; \boldsymbol{a}_i \leftarrow \mathbb{Z}_p^{n^{0.5+\epsilon}};}\\ \boldsymbol{e}_i \leftarrow \{0,1\} \ \forall \ i \in [n];}\\ Output \left( \{\boldsymbol{a}_i, \langle \boldsymbol{a}_i, \boldsymbol{s} \rangle + e_i \mod p\}_{i \in [n]}, \mathsf{G}(\boldsymbol{e}) \right)}{\frac{\mathsf{Random}_{\mathcal{A}}^G(1^n):}{\boldsymbol{s} \leftarrow \mathbb{Z}_p^{n^{0.5+\epsilon}}; \boldsymbol{a}_i \leftarrow \mathbb{Z}_p^{n^{0.5+\epsilon}};}\\ \boldsymbol{e}_i \leftarrow \{0,1\} \ \forall \ i \in [n];}\\ \boldsymbol{r} \leftarrow \{0,1\}^m;}\\ Output \left( \{\boldsymbol{a}_i, \langle \boldsymbol{a}_i, \boldsymbol{s} \rangle + e_i \mod p\}_{i \in [n]}, \boldsymbol{r} \right)}
```

{34}------------------------------------------------

Formally, we say that G satisfies LWEleak<sub> $d,\epsilon,p$ </sub> if for any ppt distinguisher A:

$$\mathsf{adv}^{\mathsf{LWEleak}_{d,\epsilon,\rho}}_{\mathsf{G},\mathcal{A}}(1^n) := |\Pr[\mathcal{A}(z_1) = 1] - \Pr[\mathcal{A}(z_2) = 1]| < \mathsf{negl}(n),$$

where  $z_1 \leftarrow \mathsf{PseudoR}^{\mathsf{G}}_{\mathcal{A}}(1^n)$  and  $z_2 \leftarrow \mathsf{Random}^{G}_{\mathcal{A}}(1^n)$ .

Further, we say the assumption is subexponetially secure if  $negl = 2^{-n^{\Omega(1)}}$ .

**Construction of** sPRG. We now describe our construction using the assumption above for parameters  $D \in \mathbb{N}$ ,  $\epsilon, \rho > 0$ , and then argue properties. Consider the following setting of parameters:

- $\lambda$  is the security parameter,
- $n(\lambda)$  is an arbitrary polynomial in  $\lambda$  which is the length parameter,
- $n' = n^{\frac{1}{(0.5+\epsilon)\cdot\lceil\frac{D}{2}\rceil}}$ ,
- $m(n') \ge n'^{\lceil \frac{D}{2} \rceil \cdot (0.5 + \epsilon) + \rho} = n^{1+\gamma}$  where  $\gamma = \frac{\rho}{(0.5 + \epsilon) \lceil \frac{D}{2} \rceil}$ . Let  $\tau = 1 + \gamma > 1$ . This will be the stretch of our sPRG.
- sPRG.PPGen $(1^{\lambda}, 1^n)$ : Run Bilinear map generator on input  $\lambda$  to sample  $\mathcal{G}_{\lambda} = (\mathbf{G}_1, \mathbf{G}_2, \mathbf{G}_1, p, P_1, P_2, e)$ . Output  $\mathsf{pp} = (\mathcal{G}_{\lambda}, p, 1^n)$ . The modulus p is implicitly a part of  $\mathcal{G}_{\lambda}$  and is a  $\mathsf{poly}(\lambda)$  bit modulus.
- $\mathsf{sPRG}.\mathsf{IdSamp}(\mathsf{pp}): \mathsf{Sample}\ \boldsymbol{a}_i \leftarrow \mathbb{Z}_p^{n'^{0.5+\epsilon}}\ \mathsf{for}\ i \in [n'].\ \mathsf{Output}\ I = (\{\boldsymbol{a}_i\}_{i \in [n']}, \mathsf{G}).$
- $\mathsf{sPRG.SdSamp}(I): \mathsf{Sample}\ \boldsymbol{s} \leftarrow_{\mathsf{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p^{n'^{0.5+\epsilon}}.$  For every  $i \in [n']$ , sample  $e_i \leftarrow \{0,1\}.$  Set  $b_i = \langle \boldsymbol{a}_i, \boldsymbol{s} \rangle + e_i \mod p$  for  $i \in [n'].$  Set  $\mathsf{P} = \boldsymbol{b}$  where  $\boldsymbol{b} = (b_1, \dots, b_{n'}).$  Compute  $\mathsf{S} = (1, \boldsymbol{s})^{\otimes \lceil \frac{D}{2} \rceil}.$  Output  $\mathsf{sd} = (\mathsf{P}, \mathsf{S}).$
- sPRG.Eval(I, sd): Output  $\sigma = G(b_1 \langle a_1, s \rangle, \dots, b_{n'} \langle a_{n'}, s \rangle) \in \{0, 1\}^{m(n')}$ . Note that every bit  $\sigma_i$  is computable by a polynomial map  $Q_I(\mathsf{P}, \mathsf{S}) \to \mathbb{Z}_p^{m(n')}$  which is a degree D map in  $\mathsf{P}$  and degree 2 map in  $\mathsf{S}$  over  $\mathbb{Z}_p$ .

We now argue properties associated with it.

**Stretch.** Note that the length of the output is  $n^{\tau}$ , where as the length of the seed is  $O(n'\log_2 p + n'^{(0.5+\epsilon)\lceil \frac{D}{2} \rceil}\log_2 p) = O(n\operatorname{poly}(\lambda))$ , because p is a  $\operatorname{poly}(\lambda)$  bit prime for some polynomial independent of n. Thus the sPRG has a stretch of  $\tau$ .

Linear Efficiency. We now compute the run time to sample sd. It computes n' LWE samples, each of dimension  $n'^{0.5+\epsilon}$ . This can be done in time  $n'^{1.5+\epsilon}$  poly $(\lambda)=n^{\frac{1}{\lceil\frac{D}{2}\rceil}}$  or  $n^{\frac{1}{\lceil\frac{D}{2}\rceil}}$  poly $(\lambda)=O(n)$  poly $(\lambda)$  if  $D\geq 5$ . It also computes S, which also takes linear time as:  $O(n'^{(0.5+\epsilon)\lceil\frac{D}{2}\rceil})$  poly $(\lambda)=O(n)$  poly $(\lambda)$ . Thus, it satisfies linear efficiency if  $D\geq 5$ .

{35}------------------------------------------------

**Pseudorandomness.** The adversary gets to see P = b along with σ = G(e). The Pseudorandomness property follows immediately from the assumption LWEleakd,,ρ.

<span id="page-35-2"></span>**Theorem 6.1.** *Let* , ρ > 0 *and* D ∈ N*. If the assumption* LWEleakD,,ρ *holds for some PRG* G*, then there exists* τ > 1 *such that the construction* sPRG *above is a secure structured seed PRG with stretch* τ *, with complexity* (arith*-*NC<sup>0</sup> , deg 2)*. If* D ≥ 5*, the* sPRG *also satisfies linear efficiency.*

# <span id="page-35-0"></span>**7 Single Ciphertext Functional Encryption with Linear Key-Gen from LWE**

In this section, for any constant ∈ (0, 1), we construct a variant of secret key functional encryption satisfying the following specifications. We denote this primitive by -1LGFE.

- (Function Class.) The function class for -1LGFE is F CIRC = {FCIRC λ,prmtr}λ,prmtr which consists of all polynomial sized Boolean circuits that takes n bit inputs, produces ` bit outputs, has depth bounded by d, and size bounded by size. Here all parameters n, `, d,size are polynomially related to the security parameter, and can be arbitrary polynomials.
- (Security.) Satisfy 1-key single ciphertext -simulation security as in Definition [4.4.](#page-24-1) Single ciphertext specifies that the number of ciphertexts is 1 and 1-key specifies that the number of secret keys is also 1 (i.e., Qsk = 1 in Definition [4.4\)](#page-24-1). Note however that the functions supported directly have many output bits.
- (Efficiency.) Satisfy *levelled* compactness as in Definition [4.7](#page-25-2) and Remark [4.8.](#page-25-3) In particular, ciphertext size is poly(λ, n, d), independent of the function size size and output length `.
- Also admits Special Structure\* defined in Definition [4.8.](#page-25-4)

We will construct such a scheme relying on the GVW predicate encryption scheme [\[GVW15\]](#page-72-9). Below we recall some preliminaries from there and then we construct -1LGFE.

### <span id="page-35-1"></span>**7.1 GVW Preliminaries**

**Predicate Encryption.** Now we recall the definition of predicate encryption scheme. A predicate encryption is a functional encryption scheme as described in Section [4,](#page-21-0) with the following differences.

- Encryptor encrypts messages of the form (attr, m) where attr ∈ {0, 1} <sup>n</sup> and m is a bit.
- The functions supported has special form C<sup>P</sup> , where P is a *predicate* in the class F CIRC λ,n,d,1,size which on input (attr, m) outputs m if P(attr) = 1 and 0 otherwise.

{36}------------------------------------------------

• (Selective) indistinguishability security of PE states that given the public key PK, the adversary cannot distinguish encryption of  $(\mathsf{attr}_0, m_0)$  from encryption of  $(\mathsf{attr}_1, m_1)$ , even if it can ask for any number of functional keys corresponding to predicates  $P_1, ..., P_\eta$ , as long as for all  $i \in [\eta]$ ,  $P_i(\mathsf{attr}_0) = P_i(\mathsf{attr}_1) = 0$ . In the security experiments, the challenges attributes  $\mathsf{attr}_0$  and  $\mathsf{attr}_1$  are chosen selectively at the beginning.

For a complete definition, please refer to [GVW15]. For our construction, we require some special properties from the predicate encryption scheme, such as efficiency, circuit homomorphism, etc. All these properties are satisfied by the construction of [GVW15], and we recall them next. The text below is a very succinct summary of the [GVW15] scheme, and will assume familiarity with some lattice preliminaries described in Section B.

<span id="page-36-0"></span>**Properties of GVW Predicate Encryption Scheme.** We first describe various algorithms and associated properties of the GVW predicate encryption scheme PE.

**Setup.** The setup algorithm takes as input the security parameter  $\lambda$ , and outputs a public key PK and a secret key SK. Namely,  $\mathsf{Setup}(1^\lambda,\mathsf{prmtr}=(n,\ell,d,\mathsf{size})) \to (\mathsf{PK},\mathsf{SK})$ , where PK contains the following parameters:

- A polynomial-length modulus  $p_1$ .  $p_1$  is of magnitude  $2^{\mathsf{poly}(\lambda,d)}$  and is set below.
- LWE matrix dimensions  $\dim_1 = \mathsf{poly}_2(\lambda, d)$  and  $\dim_2 = \mathsf{poly}_3(\lambda, d)$  which are some fixed polynomials in the security parameter.
- Uniform matrices  $\mathbf{B}_1,...,\mathbf{B}_{n'},\mathbf{A},\mathbf{D}$  where  $n'=n\cdot\mathsf{poly}(\lambda,d)$  for some polynomial poly determined below. Each matrix is in  $\mathbb{F}_{p_1}^{\dim_1\times\dim_2}$ . This is also the space of the gadget matrix  $\mathbf{G}$ .

**Encryption.** The encryption algorithm takes as input public key PK, attribute attr  $\in \{0,1\}^n$  and a message  $m \in \{0,1\}$  and outputs a two-part ciphertext,  $\operatorname{Enc}(\mathsf{PK},\mathsf{attr},m) \to (\mathsf{ct}_1,\mathsf{ct}_2)$ , which we describe in more detail below.

- The encryption algorithm first samples a secret vector s from  $\chi^{\dim_1 \times 1}$ . Here  $\chi$  is an LWE error distribution that is bounded by a fixed polynomial  $\operatorname{poly}_{\chi}(\lambda,d)$ .
- Then, it encodes attr into  $\widehat{\mathsf{attr}} = (\widehat{\mathsf{attr}}_p, \widehat{\mathsf{attr}}_s) \in \mathbb{F}_{p_1}^{n'}$  of length n', where  $\widehat{\mathsf{attr}}_p$  can and will be released in public and  $\widehat{\mathsf{attr}}_p$  must be kept secret. In the [GVW15] scheme,  $\widehat{\mathsf{attr}}_s$  is an FHE secret key and  $\widehat{\mathsf{attr}}_p$  is an FHE encryption of attr under that secret key. Below, we will only use the fact that the size of  $|\widehat{\mathsf{attr}}_p| + |\widehat{\mathsf{attr}}_s| = n' = \mathsf{poly}(\lambda, n, d) \log p_1$ , and will not rely on other details of  $(\widehat{\mathsf{attr}}_p, \widehat{\mathsf{attr}}_s)$ .
- Now  $ct_1$  is constructed using algorithm  $Enc_1(PK, s, attr)$  which does:
  - Compute  $b_i = s^T(\mathbf{B}_i + \widehat{\mathsf{attr}}_i \mathbf{G}) + e_i$  for  $i \in [n']$ . Here  $e_i \leftarrow \chi^{1 \times \dim_2}$ .

{37}------------------------------------------------

- Output  $\mathsf{ct}_1 = (\boldsymbol{b}_1, \dots, \boldsymbol{b}_{n'}, \widehat{\mathsf{attr}}_p)$
- Now ct<sub>2</sub> is constructed as follows.
  - Compute  $a = s^T \mathbf{A} + e_1$ . Here  $e_1 \leftarrow \chi^{1 \times \dim_2}$ .
  - Compute  $d = s^T D + e_2 + m |p_1/2|[1, 0, ..., 0]$ . Here  $e_2 \leftarrow \chi^{1 \times \dim_2}$ .
  - Output  $\operatorname{ct}_2 = (\boldsymbol{a}, \boldsymbol{d})$ .

Without loss of security we can assume s[1] = 1 (first component of vector s). This ensures that  $v = s^T G$  satisfies v[1] = 1.

Note: Looking ahead, in our construction of  $\epsilon$ -1LGFE, we will only use the Enc<sub>1</sub> algorithm instead of the encryption algorithm, thereby not computing  $\operatorname{ct}_2$  at all. This does not hamper security as we are just giving less information.

**Evaluation.** There are two algorithms: EvalPK operates only on the public key PK and EvalCT operates on both PK and  $ct_1$ .

- EvalPK $(C, \mathbf{B}_1, ..., \mathbf{B}_{n'}) \to \mathbf{B}_C$ : EvalPK on input  $\mathbf{B}_1, ..., \mathbf{B}_{n'}$  contained in PK, and a predicate  $C \in \mathcal{F}_{n,d,1,\mathsf{size}}$ , deterministically outputs  $\mathbf{B}_C \in \mathbb{F}_{p_1}^{\dim_1 \times \dim_2}$ .
- EvalCT(PK, C,  $\operatorname{ct}_1$ )  $\to$   $b_C$ : EvalCT on input  $\operatorname{ct}_1$  containing  $\widehat{\operatorname{attr}}_p$ ,  $\boldsymbol{b}_1, \ldots, \boldsymbol{b}_{n'}$  and a predicate C, output a scalor  $b_C \in \mathbb{F}_{p_1}$ , that satisfies the following important identity: Let  $\mathbf{B}_C[\star,1]$  denotes the first column of  $\mathbf{B}$ ;

<span id="page-37-0"></span>
$$b_C = \mathbf{s}^T \left( \mathbf{B}_C[\star, 1] + (C(\mathsf{attr}) \lfloor p_1/2 \rfloor + e_C') \mathbf{G} \right) + \mathbf{e}_C[1]$$
  
=  $\mathbf{s}^T \mathbf{B}_C[\star, 1] + C(\mathsf{attr}) \lfloor p_1/2 \rfloor + e_C$  (2)

The second identity above follows from the fact that  $(s^T\mathbf{G})[1] = 1$  and setting  $e_C = e'_C + e_C[1]$ . It satisfies that  $|e_C|/p_1 < 2^{-\lambda^c}$  for some constant c > 0. (For readers familiar with the [GVW15] PE scheme,  $e'_C$  is an error resulted from the homomorphic evaluation of the FHE scheme, which is polynomially bounded, and  $e_C[1]$  is the error resulted from the homomorphic evaluation of the ABE scheme, which is exponentially large. These details are not important for our  $\epsilon$ -1LGFE construction, which only relies on Equation (2).)

**Remark 7.1.** The algorithms described above are already close enough to imply a construction of  $\epsilon$ -1LGFE where the encryption is simply  $\mathsf{Enc}_1$  producing  $\mathsf{ct}_1$  as above, the master secret key is s, and the function key for any function C could just be computed as  $\mathsf{sk}_C = \langle s, \mathbf{B}[\star, 1] \rangle + e$ , where e is chosen freshly from some bounded smudging distribution. This leads to the evaluation-decryption equation:

$$\mathsf{EvalCT}(\mathsf{PK}, C, \mathsf{ct}_1) - \mathsf{sk}_C = C(\mathsf{attr}) \cdot \lceil p_1/2 \rceil + e_C - e$$

The only problem is that  $e_C$  is not polynomially bounded, and thus this does not fit in the requirements for an  $\epsilon$ -1LGFE scheme, which requires both e and  $e_C - e$  to be polynomially bounded. To fix this issue, we introduce the following algorithm, which rounds  $b_C \in \mathbb{F}_{p_1}$  to another modulus p so that the rounded version of the error  $e'_C$  is polynomially bounded.

{38}------------------------------------------------

**Rounding-Evaluation.** We now describe a procedure of rounding evaluation, RoundEval, which can be done publicly. RoundEval(PK, C,  $\mathsf{ct}_1, p$ ), takes as input PK,  $\mathsf{ct}_1 = (\widehat{\mathsf{attr}}_p, \boldsymbol{b}_1, \dots, \boldsymbol{b}_{n'})$ , a predicate C, and a modulus  $p < p_1$ , and does the following:

- 1. Run EvalCT(PK, C, ct<sub>1</sub>)  $\rightarrow b_C$ .
- 2. Compute  $b'_C = \lceil p/p_1 \cdot b_C \rfloor$ . Namely multiply  $b_C$  with  $p/p_1$  over the reals and then take the nearest integer. Output  $b'_C \in \mathbb{F}_p$ .

Now we observe the structure of  $b'_C$ , relying on the lemma proven about rounding in [BGV12] (see lemma 1 of the paper).

<span id="page-38-2"></span>**Theorem 7.1.** For any primes p and  $p_1 > p$ , given that

- $b_C = s^T \mathbf{B}_C[\star, 1] + C(\mathsf{attr}) \lfloor p_1/2 \rfloor + e_C$  where s is sampled from the distribution  $\chi^{\dim_1}$ , and
- $\chi$  is a polynomially bounded distribution, bounded by,  $\operatorname{poly}_{\chi}(\lambda,d)$ ,

<span id="page-38-1"></span>
$$b'_{C} = \mathbf{s}^{T} \cdot \mathbf{d}_{C} + C(\mathsf{attr}) \lfloor p/2 \rfloor + e'_{C} + e_{\mathsf{rnd}}$$

$$where \ \mathbf{d}_{C} = \mathsf{Round}_{p_{1} \to p}(\mathbf{B}_{C}[\star, 1]), \ e'_{C} = \mathsf{Round}_{p_{1} \to p}(e_{C})$$
(3)

where  $\mathsf{Round}_{p_1 \to p}(v) = \lceil p \cdot v / p_1 \rfloor$  and  $e_{\mathrm{rnd}}$  is the rounding error satisfying  $|e_{\mathrm{rnd}}| < \dim_1 \cdot \mathsf{poly}_\chi(\lambda, d) + 5$ .

Below, we will refer to  $b_C'$  as the output ciphertext from the RoundEval procedure, and error  $=(e_C'+e_{\mathrm{rnd}})$  the noise or error in this output ciphertext.

# <span id="page-38-0"></span>7.2 Setting Parameters

Now we set the parameters that will be used in our constructions of  $\epsilon$ -1LGFE. All these parameters can be realized using standard LWE assumption with subexponential approximation factors.

- $\dim_1$  and  $\dim_2$  are chosen as in the [GVW15] predicate encryption scheme.
- The error distribution  $\chi$  is a truncated discrete Gaussian distribution bounded by  $\operatorname{poly}_{\chi}(\lambda,d).$
- The prime p is exactly the order of a prime-order bilinear pairing group, which is sub-exponentially large in the security parameter. (More precisely,  $\{p_{\lambda}\}$  is exactly the order of a sequence of prime-order bilinear pairing groups.)
- The prime modulus  $p_1$  is so that part of the error  $e'_C$  as shown in Equation (3) in the output ciphertext from the RoundEval procedure is bounded by  $\lambda$ .

{39}------------------------------------------------

By the properties of the PE scheme of [GVW15], the error resulted by evaluating a predicate grows exponentially with the security parameter  $\lambda$  and the depth d of the predicate.

$$|e_C| \le (\dim_1 + \dim_2)^{\mathsf{poly}_{\mathsf{PE}}(\lambda,d)} \cdot \mathsf{poly}_{\chi}(\lambda,d)$$

where poly<sub>PE</sub> is a fixed polynomial decided by the PE scheme.

Thus  $p_1$  must satisfy:

$$|e_C'| \leq \left\lceil \frac{p \cdot (\dim_1 + \dim_2)^{\mathsf{poly}_{\mathsf{PE}}(\lambda,d)} \cdot \mathsf{poly}_{\chi}(\lambda,d)}{p_1} \right\rceil \leq \lambda$$

Set  $p_1$  to any prime satisfying the above inequality, of magnitude:

$$p_1 = \Theta \left( p(\dim_1 + \dim_2)^{\mathsf{poly}_{\mathsf{PE}}(\lambda,d)} \cdot \mathsf{poly}_{\chi}(\lambda,d) \right) ,$$

The bit length of the prime  $p_1$  is  $\log p + \mathsf{poly}(\lambda, d) = \mathsf{poly}'(\lambda, d)$ .

By the above way of setting parameters, we have that the error in the output ciphertexts by RoundEval is bounded by a fixed polynomial in  $\lambda$ .

<span id="page-39-1"></span>**Claim 7.1.** Under the above way of setting parameters, there is a universal polynomial  $Bnd(\cdot)$ , such that, for every  $\lambda \in \mathbb{N}$ , predicate  $C \in \mathcal{F}_{n,d,1,\text{size}}$ , every input  $x \in \{0,1\}^n$ , every PK in the support of  $Setup(1^{\lambda}, prmtr = (n, \ell, d, size))$ , ciphertext  $ct_1$  in the support of  $Enc_1$ , and every output ciphertext  $b'_C$  in the support of  $CoundEval(PK, C, ct_1, p)$ , the error error in  $b'_C$  satisfies  $|error| \leq Bnd(\lambda, d)$ .

*Proof.* The claim follows from the fact that error  $=e'_C+e_{\mathrm{rnd}}$ , where the former is bounded by  $\lambda$  due to how the modulus  $p_1$  is set, and the latter is bounded by  $\dim_1 \cdot \mathsf{poly}_\chi(\lambda,d) + 5$  by Theorem 7.1. Therefore, we set simply  $\mathsf{Bnd} = \lambda + \dim_1 \cdot \mathsf{poly}_\chi(\lambda,d) + 5$ .

**Efficiency:** We particularly remark about the ciphertext size  $|\mathsf{ct}_1|$  of the PE scheme, which will dictate the ciphertext size of our  $\epsilon$ -1LGFE below. The  $\mathsf{ct}_1 = (\boldsymbol{b}_1, \cdots, \boldsymbol{b}_{n'}, \widehat{\mathsf{attr}}_p)$ , where each  $\boldsymbol{b}_i \in \mathbb{Z}_{p_1}^{1 \times \dim_2}$ . By our parameter setting above  $p_1$  has  $\mathsf{poly}(\lambda, d)$  bits. Therefore  $n' = |\widehat{\mathsf{attr}}_p| + |\widehat{\mathsf{attr}}_s| = \mathsf{poly}(\lambda, n) \log p_1 = \mathsf{poly}(\lambda, n, d)$ . Since further  $\dim_2 = \mathsf{poly}(\lambda, d)$ , the total size of  $\mathsf{ct}_1$  is bounded by  $\mathsf{poly}(\lambda, n, d)$ .

#### <span id="page-39-0"></span>7.3 Construction of $\epsilon$ -1LGFE

We adapt the predicate encryption of [GVW15] with additional rounding to our the construction of  $\epsilon$ -1LGFE, which is described in Figure 7.3. To avoid confusion, every algorithm X of the PE scheme will be denoted as PE.X.

Observe that correctness and syntactic properties are immediate due to the properties of the predicate encryption scheme. For completeness, we sketch these below.

{40}------------------------------------------------

```
\epsilon-1LGFE.PPGen(1^{\lambda}, \mathsf{prmtr} = (n, d, \ell, \mathsf{size})):
```

- Set p to be the prime order of a fixed bilinear pairing group for security parameter  $\lambda$ .
- Run PE.Setup $(1^{\lambda}, prmtr) \rightarrow (PK, SK)$ , where  $PK = ((\mathbf{B}_1, ..., \mathbf{B}_{n'}), p_1)$ . Set pp = (p, prmtr, PK) (and discard SK.)

```
\epsilon-1LGFE.Setup(pp) : Sample s \leftarrow \chi^{\dim_1 \times 1}. Set msk = s.
```

 $\epsilon$ -1LGFE.Enc(s, m): Run PE.Enc $_1(PK, s, m) \to ct$ . Output ct.

 $\epsilon$ -1LGFE.KeyGen(s,C):C has  $\ell$ -bit outputs. For every  $j\in[\ell]$ ,

- set  $C_j$  to be the depth d circuit that computes the j'th output bit,
- compute PE.EvalPK(PK,  $C_j$ )  $\to$   $\mathbf{B}_{C_j}$ , and  $\mathbf{d}_{C_j} = \mathsf{Round}_{p_1 \to p}(\mathbf{B}_{C_j}[\star, 1])$ , the rounded version of the first column of  $\mathbf{B}_{C_j}$ ,
- compute  $\operatorname{sk}_{C_j} = \langle \boldsymbol{d}_{C_j}, \boldsymbol{s} \rangle + e_j \bmod p$  where  $e_j \leftarrow [-\mathsf{Bnd}', \mathsf{Bnd}'] \cap \mathbb{Z}$  and  $\mathsf{Bnd}' = \mathsf{Bnd}'(\lambda)$  is a fixed polynomial set to:  $\mathsf{Bnd}'(\lambda) = \lceil \frac{\mathsf{Bnd} \cdot \ell}{1 \epsilon} \rceil$ .

Output secret key  $\mathsf{sk}_C = \{\mathsf{sk}_{C_j}\}_{j \in [\ell]}$ 

 $\frac{\epsilon\text{-1LGFE.Dec}(\mathsf{sk}_C,\mathsf{ct}): \text{For every } j \in [\ell] \text{, compute PE.RoundEval}(\mathsf{PK},C_j,\mathsf{ct},p) \to b'_{C_j},}{\text{and if } z_j = (b'_{C_j} - \mathsf{sk}_{C_j}) \bmod p \in [-p/4,p/4], \text{ set } y_j = 0, \text{ and otherwise set } y_j = 1.}$  Output y.

Figure 1: Construction of  $\epsilon$ -1LGFE.

{41}------------------------------------------------

**Correctness:** If the setup, encryption, and the key generation are done honestly, then from the properties of the PE scheme, the following happens. Let ct denote a ciphertext encrypting  $m \in \{0,1\}^n$ , and let  $\mathsf{sk}_C = \{\mathsf{sk}_{C_j}\}$  be a function secret key for a circuit  $C \in \mathcal{F}^{\mathsf{CIRC}}_{\lambda,\mathsf{prmtr}}$ . Then, for every  $j \in [\ell]$ ,

$$b'_{C_j} = \mathsf{PE.RoundEval}(\mathsf{PK}, C_j, \mathsf{ct}, p) = \boldsymbol{s}^T \cdot \boldsymbol{d}_{C_j} + C_j(m) \lfloor p/2 \rfloor + \mathsf{error}_j$$
  
 $\mathsf{sk}_C = \boldsymbol{s}^T \cdot \boldsymbol{d}_{C_j} + e_j, \qquad \text{where } \boldsymbol{d}_{C_j} = \mathsf{Round}_{p_1 \to p}(\mathbf{B}_{C_j}[\star, 1]) \ .$ 

Moreover,  $e_j$  is bounded by a polynomial Bnd', and by Claim 7.1, error<sub>j</sub> is bounded by another polynomial Bnd. Decryption computes

$$z_j = b'_{C_j} - \operatorname{sk}_{C_j} \mod p = C_j(m)\lceil p/2 \rceil + \operatorname{error}_j', \quad \operatorname{error}_j' = e_j - \operatorname{error}_j.$$

Because p is subexponentially large and error' is polynomially bounded, if  $C_j(m) = 0$  then  $z \in [-p/4, p/4]$  and otherwise not. Therefore, the decryption algorithm recovers every bit of the output correctly.

**Special Structure\*.** It is easy to verify that our  $\epsilon$ -1LGFE has the special structure\*: We have four polynomials  $q_1$ ,  $\dim_1$ ,  $\operatorname{Bnd}'$ ,  $\operatorname{Bnd} + \operatorname{Bnd}'$ , such that, all required properties are satisfied.

- (PP Syntax.) The pp contains modulus p, which is a  $q_1 = poly(\lambda)$ -bit prime.
- (Linear secret key Structure.) The master secret key is a vector in  $s \in \mathbb{Z}_{p_1}^{\dim_1}$ . For any function  $C \in \mathcal{F}_{\lambda,n,\ell,d,\mathrm{size}}^{\mathrm{CIRC}}$ , the functional secret key  $\mathrm{sk}_C = \{\mathrm{sk}_{C_j}\}$  and each  $\mathrm{sk}_{C_j}$  satisfies the special form of  $\mathrm{sk}_{C_j} = \langle \boldsymbol{d}_{C_j}, \boldsymbol{s} \rangle + e_j \mod p$  where  $e_j \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} [-\mathrm{Bnd}', \mathrm{Bnd}'] \cap \mathbb{Z}$ . Finally,  $d_{C_j}$  is deterministically and efficiently computed from the PK contained in pp and f.
- (Linear + Round Decryption with polynomial decryption error.) The procedure PE.RoundEval(PK,  $C_j$ , ct, p) computes output ciphertext  $b'_{C_j}$ , such that,  $|b'_{C_j} \langle \boldsymbol{d}_{C_j}, \boldsymbol{s} \rangle C_j(m) \lfloor p/2 \rfloor \rangle| \leq \text{Bnd}' + \text{Bnd}$ .

**Remark 7.2.** Note that above we consider  $e_j$  to be sampled from [-Bnd', Bnd'] but equivalently we could have considered it being sampled from [0, 2Bnd'] (which was the requirement in Definition 4.8). This is because the keys can be transformed from one into other by adding a fixed constant Bnd'.

**Leveled Compactness:** Observe that the ciphertext ct for a message m is exactly the ct<sub>1</sub> generated by PE.Enc<sub>1</sub>(PK, s, m), which as analyzed in Section 7.1 is poly( $\lambda$ , n, d).

# <span id="page-41-0"></span>7.4 Single-Ciphertext $\epsilon$ -simulation security

For any fixed constant  $\epsilon \in (0,1)$ , we now prove that the construction in Figure 7.3 is 1-key single-ciphertext  $\epsilon$ -simulation secure.

<span id="page-41-1"></span>**Theorem 7.2.** Assuming LWE assumption holds for the parameters described in Section 7.2, the construction  $\epsilon$ -1LGFE in Figure 7.3 satisfies 1-key single-ciphertext  $\epsilon$ -simulation security.

{42}------------------------------------------------

# $\underline{\mathsf{Real}}_{\mathcal{A}_{-}}^{\epsilon\text{-1LGFE}}(1^{\lambda}) :$

- 1. Challenge:  $(m, C) \leftarrow \mathcal{A}_1(1^{\lambda}, \mathsf{prmtr} = (n, d, \ell, \mathsf{size}))$  (where  $n, d, \ell$ , and size are polynomials,  $m \in \{0, 1\}^n$ , and  $C \in \mathcal{F}_{\lambda, n, \ell, d, \mathsf{size}}^{\mathsf{CIRC}}$ ). Below  $n = n(\lambda)$ ,  $\ell = \ell(\lambda)$ ,  $\ell = \ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , and  $\ell = \ell(\lambda)$ , and  $\ell = \ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , and  $\ell = \ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , and  $\ell = \ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , and  $\ell = \ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , and  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , and  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , and  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , and  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , and prmtr =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , and prmtr =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , and prmtr =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ .
- 2. Generate pp: pp  $\leftarrow \epsilon$ -1LGFE.PPGen $(1^{\lambda}, prmtr)$ , where pp = (p, prmtr, PK) and PK  $= ((\mathbf{B}_1, ..., \mathbf{B}_{n'}), p_1)$ .
- 3. Generate master secret key:  $s \leftarrow \epsilon$ -1LGFE.Setup(pp), where  $s \leftarrow \chi^{\dim_1 \times 1}$ .
- 4. Generate the challenge ciphertext: ct  $\leftarrow \epsilon$ -1LGFE.Enc(s, m), where ct is generated using PE.Enc<sub>1</sub>(PK, s, m).
- 5. Generate the challenge secret key:  $\mathsf{sk}_C \leftarrow \epsilon\text{-1LGFE.KeyGen}(\boldsymbol{s},C)$ , where  $\mathsf{sk}_C = \{\mathsf{sk}_{C_j}\}_{j\in[\ell]}$ , and  $\mathsf{sk}_{C_j} = \langle \boldsymbol{d}_{C_j}, \boldsymbol{s}\rangle + e_j \bmod p$ , for  $\mathbf{B}_{C_j} \leftarrow \mathsf{PE.EvalPK}(\mathsf{PK},C_j)$ ,  $\boldsymbol{d}_{C_j} = \mathsf{Round}_{p_1 \to p}(\mathbf{B}_{C_j}[\star,1])$ , and  $e_j \leftarrow [-\mathsf{Bnd}',\mathsf{Bnd}'] \cap \mathbb{Z}$ .

Output  $\beta \leftarrow \mathcal{A}_2(\{\mathsf{pp},\mathsf{ct},\mathsf{sk}_C\})$ .

<span id="page-42-0"></span>Figure 2: The real-world  $\epsilon$ -simulation security game of  $\epsilon$ -1LGFE.

*Proof.* By definition 4.3, to prove single ciphertext  $\epsilon$ -simulation security, we need to show a PPT simulator S, such that for all stateful PPT adversaries  $A = (A_1, A_2)$ , there is a negligible function negl, such that, for any security parameters  $\lambda \in \mathbb{N}$ ,

$$\mathsf{adv}^{\mathsf{SIM}}_{\epsilon\text{-1LGFE},\mathcal{A}}(1^{\lambda}) := |\Pr[1 \leftarrow \mathsf{Real}^{\epsilon\text{-1LGFE}}_{\mathcal{A}}(1^{\lambda})] - \Pr[1 \leftarrow \mathsf{Ideal}^{\epsilon\text{-1LGFE}}_{\mathcal{A},\mathcal{S}}(1^{\lambda})]| < \mathsf{negl}(\lambda),$$

where the Real experiment is described in Figure 7.4, and the ideal experiment Ideal defined in figure 7.4, which also defines out simulator S. We show that the view of A in the real and ideal experiments are indistinguishable (and hence has the same probability of outputting 1) through two intermediate hybrids:

**Hybrid**  $H_1$ : This hybrid is identical to the real world experiment except for how  $\mathsf{sk}_C$  is generated: by construction of  $\epsilon$ -1LGFE, it holds that

$$\begin{split} b'_{C_j} &= \mathsf{PE.RoundEval}(\mathsf{PK}, C_j, \mathsf{ct}, p) = \boldsymbol{s}^T \cdot \boldsymbol{d}_{C_j} + y_j \lfloor p/2 \rfloor + \mathsf{error}_j \\ \mathsf{sk}_C &= \boldsymbol{s}^T \cdot \boldsymbol{d}_{C_j} + e_j \\ &\quad \text{where } \boldsymbol{d}_{C_j} = \mathsf{Round}_{p_1 \to p}(\mathbf{B}_{C_j}[\star, 1]) \text{ and by Claim 7.1 } |\mathsf{error}_j| < \mathsf{Bnd} \;. \end{split}$$

Therefore,  $H_1$  replace Step 5 in the real world by the following equivalent way of generating  $sk_C$ :

5'. For every j, compute  $b'_{C_j} = \mathsf{PE}.\mathsf{RoundEval}(\mathsf{PK}, C_j, \mathsf{ct}, p)$ , then figure out the noise  $\mathsf{error}_j$  in  $b'_{C_j}$  using the master secret key s, and  $\mathsf{set}$ 

$$\mathsf{sk}_{C_j} = b'_{C_j} - y_j \lfloor p/2 \rfloor + \mathsf{error}'_j$$
, where  $\mathsf{error}'_j = (e_j - \mathsf{error}_j), \ e_j \leftarrow [-\mathsf{Bnd}', \mathsf{Bnd}'] \cap \mathbb{Z}$ .

{43}------------------------------------------------

# $\mathsf{Ideal}_{\mathcal{A},\mathcal{S}}^{\epsilon\text{-1LGFE}}(1^{\lambda})$ :

- 1. Challenge:  $(m, C) \leftarrow \mathcal{A}_1(1^{\lambda}, \mathsf{prmtr} = (n, d, \ell, \mathsf{size}))$  (where  $n, d, \ell$ , and size are polynomials,  $m \in \{0, 1\}^n$ , and  $C \in \mathcal{F}_{\lambda, n, \ell, d, \mathsf{size}}^{\mathsf{CIRC}}$ ). Below  $n = n(\lambda)$ ,  $\ell = \ell(\lambda)$ ,  $\ell = \ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , and  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , and  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , and  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , and  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , and  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , and  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , and  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , and  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , and  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , and  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , and prmtr =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , and prmtr =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , and prmtr =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , and prmtr =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ , size =  $\ell(\lambda)$ .
- 2. Generate pp: pp  $\leftarrow \epsilon$ -1LGFE.PPGen $(1^{\lambda}, prmtr)$ , where pp = (p, prmtr, PK) and PK  $= ((\mathbf{B}_1, ..., \mathbf{B}_{n'}), p_1)$ .
- 3. Sampling Bernouli Variables: Sample Bernouli random variable v as follows. For each  $j \in [\ell]$ , sample  $v_j \leftarrow \operatorname{Bernouli}(\sigma)$  for  $\sigma = \frac{2(\operatorname{Bnd'-Bnd})+1}{2\operatorname{Bnd'}+1}$ ;  $v_j$  indicates whether the good event  $\operatorname{good}_j$  occurs:

Event  $good_j: e_j - error_j \in GoodRng$ , where GoodRng = [-Bnd' + Bnd, Bnd' - Bnd].

Then set  $v := \min_{j \in [\ell]}(v_j)$ , indicating whether good<sub>j</sub> occurs for all j.

By Claim 7.2, the probability  $\sigma$  of  $\operatorname{good}_j$  occurring is at least  $\sigma \geq 1 - \frac{(1-\epsilon)}{\ell}$ . Thus the probability that v is 1 is at least  $\Pr[v=1] \geq 1 - \ell(1-\sigma) \geq \epsilon$ .

Next, the simulator  $\mathcal{S}=(\widetilde{\operatorname{Enc}},\widetilde{\operatorname{KeyGen}})$  simulates the ciphertext and secret keys: If v=1,  $\mathcal{S}$  receives from the oracle w=(C,C(m)), and otherwise w=(C,m). Sample  $s\leftarrow\chi^{\dim_1\times 1}$ . Let  $\operatorname{td}=s$ .

- 4. Simulate the challenge ciphertext Enc(td, w): If) v = 1, simulate the ciphertext  $ct \leftarrow PE.Enc_1(PK, s, 0)$ , and If) v = 0, generate the ciphertext of m honestly  $ct \leftarrow PE.Enc_1(PK, s, m)$ .
- 5. Simulate the challenge secret key KeyGen(td, w, C):  $sk_C = \{sk_{C_j}\}$ , where for every  $j \in [\ell]$ ,

$$b'_{C_j} = \mathsf{PE}.\mathsf{RoundEval}(\mathsf{PK}, C_j, \mathsf{ct}, p) \;, \quad \mathsf{sk}_{C_j} = b'_{C_j} - y_j \lceil p/2 \rceil + \mathsf{error}'_j \;,$$

and the noise  $error'_j$  is sampled as below:

- If  $v_j = 1$ , error $'_j \leftarrow \mathsf{GoodRng}$ ;
- if  $v_j = 0$  (which implies v = 0), first figure out the error in  $b'_{C_j}$ ,

$$b'_{C_j} = \langle \boldsymbol{d}_{C_j}, \boldsymbol{s} \rangle + y_j \lceil p/2 \rceil + \operatorname{error}_j, \quad |\operatorname{error}_j| < \operatorname{Bnd},$$

set  $\operatorname{error}_j' = e_j - \operatorname{error}_j$ , by rejection sampling  $e_j \leftarrow [-\mathsf{Bnd}', \mathsf{Bnd}'] \cap \mathbb{Z}$  conditioned on  $\operatorname{error}_j' \not\in \mathsf{GoodRng}$ .

Output  $\beta \leftarrow \mathcal{A}_2(\{\mathsf{pp},\mathsf{ct},\mathsf{sk}_C\})$ .

Figure 3: The ideal-world  $\epsilon$ -simulation security game of  $\epsilon$ -1LGFE, and the definition of simulator S. The red steps are steps that are different from the real execution.

{44}------------------------------------------------

By construction of  $\epsilon$ -1LGFE,  $H_1$  and the real world generate identically distributed secret keys. Therefore the probabilities that  $\mathcal{A}$  outputs 1 are identical in these two worlds.

Next, we observe two properties of the noise  $\operatorname{error}_j' = e_j - \operatorname{error}_j$  that will be instrumental later. Denote by  $\operatorname{good}_j$  the event that  $\operatorname{error}_j' \in \operatorname{GoodRng} = [-\operatorname{Bnd}' + \operatorname{Bnd}, \operatorname{Bnd}' - \operatorname{Bnd}]$ . We show that the probability that  $\operatorname{good}_j$  occurs is independent of the value of  $\operatorname{error}_j$ , and the distribution of  $\operatorname{error}_j$  conditioned on  $\operatorname{good}_j$  is uniform over  $\operatorname{GoodRng}$ .

<span id="page-44-0"></span>**Claim 7.2.** *Let* Bnd' > Bnd *be positive integers and* GoodRng = [-Bnd' + Bnd, Bnd' - Bnd]. *For every*  $error \in [-Bnd, Bnd]$ ,

$$\Pr[\mathsf{error'} = e - \mathsf{error} \in \mathsf{GoodRng}] = \frac{2(\mathsf{Bnd'} - \mathsf{Bnd}) + 1}{2\mathsf{Bnd'} + 1} = \sigma \geq 1 - \frac{1 - \epsilon}{\ell}$$

where  $e \leftarrow [-\mathsf{Bnd'}, \mathsf{Bnd'}] \cap \mathbb{Z}$ , and the distribution of error' conditioned on error'  $\in \mathsf{GoodRng}$  is uniform over  $\mathsf{GoodRng}$ .

*Proof.* The probability of error'  $\in$  GoodRng is the same as the probability of  $e \in [-\mathsf{Bnd'} + \mathsf{Bnd} + \mathsf{error}, \mathsf{Bnd'} - \mathsf{Bnd} - \mathsf{error}] \subseteq [-\mathsf{Bnd'}, \mathsf{Bnd'}] \cap \mathbb{Z}$ . Since e is uniformly distributed in  $[-\mathsf{Bnd'}, \mathsf{Bnd'}] \cap \mathbb{Z}$ , this probability is exactly  $\sigma$ .

Furthermore, for any value  $y \in \mathsf{GoodRng}$ ,  $\Pr[\mathsf{error'} = y | \mathsf{error'} \in \mathsf{GoodRng}]$  is the same as  $\Pr[e = y + \mathsf{error} | e \in [-\mathsf{Bnd'} + \mathsf{Bnd} + \mathsf{error}, \mathsf{Bnd'} - \mathsf{Bnd} - \mathsf{error}]] = 1/(2(\mathsf{Bnd'} - \mathsf{Bnd}) + 1)$ . Therefore, the conditional distribution is uniform.

Finally, the lower bound on the probability follows immediately from the way that  $\mathsf{Bnd}'$  is set in the construction; see Figure 7.3.

Second, observe that given  $\operatorname{error}_j$ , we can efficiently sample  $\operatorname{error}_j'$  conditioned on  $\operatorname{good}_j$  not occurring using rejection sampling: Keep sampling  $e_j \leftarrow [-\operatorname{Bnd}',\operatorname{Bnd}'] \cap \mathbb{Z}$  till  $\operatorname{error}_j' = e_j - \operatorname{error}_j \not\in \operatorname{GoodRng}$ . This rejection sampling is efficient since the probability of  $\operatorname{good}_i$  not occurring is  $\frac{2\operatorname{Bnd}}{2\operatorname{Bnd}'+1}$  which is polynomial. Given these properties, we are now ready to present the next hybrid.

**Hybrid**  $H_2$ : This hybrid is identical to  $H_1$ , except that when generating  $sk_C$ , the noise error'<sub>j</sub> is sampled in two cases:

5". For every 
$$j$$
, compute  $b'_{C_j} = \mathsf{PE}.\mathsf{RoundEval}(\mathsf{PK}, C_j, \mathsf{ct}, p)$ , and set

$$\mathsf{sk}_{C_j} = b'_{C_j} - y_j \lfloor p/2 \rfloor + \mathsf{error}'_j$$
.

Here, error'<sub>j</sub> is sampled as follows: Sample indicator random variable  $v_j \leftarrow \text{Bernouli}(\sigma)$ .

- If  $v_j = 1$ , error'<sub>i</sub>  $\leftarrow$  GoodRng;
- if  $v_j = 0$ , figure out the noise error<sub>j</sub> in  $b'_{C_j}$  using the master secret key s, and set error'<sub>j</sub> =  $e_j$  error<sub>j</sub>, by rejection sampling  $e_j \leftarrow [-\mathsf{Bnd'}, \mathsf{Bnd'}] \cap \mathbb{Z}$  conditioned on error'<sub>j</sub>  $\not\in \mathsf{GoodRng}$ .

It follows from our observations above that the  $H_1$  and  $H_2$  generate the same dis-

{45}------------------------------------------------

tribution of errors error<sup>j</sup> 's and hence the probability that A outputs 1 is identical in these two hybrids. Furthermore, also note that H<sup>2</sup> is efficient since rejection sampling is efficient.

**The simulation world:** The simulation world Ideal described in Figure [7.4](#page-42-0) is identical to H<sup>2</sup> except that *i)* it samples all indicator variables v<sup>j</sup> in Step 3, and set v = min<sup>j</sup> (v<sup>j</sup> ) to be the indicator variable for the event that all v<sup>j</sup> 's are 1. *ii)* the main difference is that when v = 1, we switch the challenge ciphertext ct from encrypting m to encrypting 0. The first change does not change the distribution of the simulated secret key and ciphertext. The second change produces an indistinguishable ciphertext since when v = 1, every v<sup>j</sup> = 1 and error<sup>0</sup> j is sampled uniformly from GoodRng without using the master secret key s. Therefore, changing the encrypted value is indistinguishable.

Finally, we observe that the simulator is well-formed: Whenever v = 1, it simulates using only y = C(m), and when v = 0, it simulates using C, m. Furthermore, the probability that v = 1 is at least 1 − `(1 − σ) ≥ as required.

**Remark 7.3** (On Subexponential Security)**.** Above, we proved polynomial security of -1LGFE, that is the real and ideal worlds are indistinguishable to polynomial-sized adversaries with negligible distinguishing advantage. If relying on the subexponential indistinguishability of LWE, we can prove that real and ideal worlds are subexponentially indistinguishable. This is because the views of A in the real world, hybrid H1, and H<sup>2</sup> are identically distributed; and the views in H<sup>2</sup> and ideal worlds are subexponentially indistinguishable by subexponential LWE.

#### <span id="page-45-0"></span>**8 Our** (arith**-**NC<sup>1</sup> , deg 2)**-PHFE from Pairings**

In Fig[.8.2](#page-47-0) we present a Partially-Hiding FE (PHFE) for the functionality F PHFE = dn,`,w∈poly{FPHFE λ,n(λ),`(λ),w(λ) where for all λ ∈ N, F PHFE λ,n(λ),`(λ),w(λ) denotes the set of all functions defined relative a sequence of pairing group G<sup>λ</sup> of the form G<sup>λ</sup> = (G1, G2, G<sup>T</sup> , p, P1, P2, e) (see the definition of pairing group in Section [3\)](#page-19-0) as follows. Each function in F PHFE λ,prmtr is represented by a tuple (f 0 , . . . , f`+1) such that for all inputs (x, y, z) ∈ (Z n p ) 3 , it outputs <sup>h</sup> f <sup>0</sup> Q` <sup>i</sup>=1 f i (x)f `+1(y ⊗ z) i T ∈ G<sup>T</sup> , where f <sup>0</sup> ∈ Z 1×w p , f i (x) ∈ Z w×w p for all i ∈ [d], f `+1(y ⊗ z) ∈ Z w p , and all functions f i for i > 0 are linear maps. The computation is performed in the exponent of a generator of the cyclic group G<sup>T</sup> , of order p. This model of computation captures functions f of the form: f(x, y, z) = w(g(x), y ⊗ z), where w is a multilinear degree two polynomial (with degree one in y⊗z) and g is a matrix branching program of width w and length ` over Zp. By Barrington's theorem, for sufficiently large `, w, log(p) = poly(λ), it also contains the case when g is a Boolean NC<sup>1</sup> circuit (x being restricted to be a binary vector in this case). Note that to realize Boolean NC<sup>1</sup> circuits, we need each function f i to be affine, which can be ensured by setting, say, x<sup>1</sup> = 1.

We give a modular construction of PHFE for the functionality F PHFE prmtr in Section [8.2](#page-47-0) that builds upon inner-product FE, defined in Section [8.1.](#page-46-0) Our construction is linearly 

{46}------------------------------------------------

efficient as per Definition 4.6. That is, the ciphertext is computed by a circuit of size at  $\mathsf{most} n \cdot \mathsf{poly}(\lambda)$  for a fixed polynomial, where  $\lambda$  denotes the security parameter and n is dimension of the vectors being encrypted. As such, our PHFE can be used to build general purpose FE in Section 9. Finally, we build the concrete inner-product FE scheme that underlies our PHFE in Section 8.3. The security of all of our constructions rely on standard assumptions in pairing groups.

### <span id="page-46-0"></span>8.1 Ingredients: Inner-Product FE

We define the functionality  $\mathcal{F}^{\mathsf{IPFE}} = \bigcup_{\dim \in \mathsf{poly}} \{\mathcal{F}^{\mathsf{IPFE}}_{\dim,\lambda}\}_{\lambda}$  where for all dimensions  $\dim \in \mathsf{poly}(\lambda)$ , all  $\lambda \in \mathbb{N}$ ,  $\mathcal{F}^{\mathsf{IPFE}}_{\dim,\lambda}$  is the set of function  $f: \mathbf{G}^{\dim}_1 \to \mathbf{G}_T$ , described by a vector  $[y]_2 \in \mathbf{G}^{\dim}_2$ , such that given as input  $[x]_1 \in \mathbf{G}^{\dim}_1$ , outputs  $[x^\top y]_T \in \mathbf{G}_T$ . We define the functionality  $\mathcal{F}^{\mathsf{ipfe'}}$  similarly except the inputs x are in  $\mathbb{Z}^{\dim}$  instead of  $\mathbf{G}^{\dim}_1$ . To build an FE for  $\mathcal{F}^{\mathsf{PHFE}}$ , we rely on a private-key IND-function-hiding FE  $\widehat{\mathsf{IPFE}}$  for the functionality  $\mathcal{F}^{\mathsf{IPFE}}$  and an FE  $\widehat{\mathsf{IPFE}}$  for the functionality  $\mathcal{F}^{\mathsf{IPFE'}}$ . We only require that the scheme  $\widehat{\mathsf{IPFE}}$  satisfies a simulation security that is slightly weaker than defined Definition 4.3, in the sense that the simulator generates the functional secret keys for a function  $[y]_2$  only knowing the output  $[x^\top y]_2$  in  $G_2$  or  $[x^\top y]_1$  in  $G_1$ , as opposed to  $G_T$ , where x denotes the challenge (see Definition 8.1).

<span id="page-46-1"></span>**Definition 8.1** (Weak simulation security). Let FE be an FE scheme for the functionality  $\mathcal{F}^{\mathsf{IPFE'}}$  defined above. We say that FE is weakly simulation secure if there exists a PPT simulator  $\mathcal{S} := (\widetilde{\mathsf{Setup}}, \widetilde{\mathsf{Enc}}, \widetilde{\mathsf{KeyGen}}_1, \widetilde{\mathsf{KeyGen}}_2)$  such that for all  $\dim \in \mathsf{poly}$ ,  $\lambda \in \mathbb{N}$ , all  $\mathsf{prmtr} = (w, \ell, n)$ , all  $\mathsf{pp}$  in the support of  $\mathsf{PPGen}(1^\lambda, \mathsf{prmtr})$ , all  $(\widetilde{\mathsf{pk}}, \widetilde{\mathsf{msk}})$  in the support of  $\widetilde{\mathsf{Setup}}(1^\lambda)$ , all  $y \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{\dim}$ ,  $v \in \mathbb{Z}_p$ , the following are identically distributed:

$$\widetilde{\mathsf{KeyGen}}_1(\widetilde{\mathsf{msk}},[\boldsymbol{y}]_1,[v]_1)$$
 and  $\widetilde{\mathsf{KeyGen}}_2(\widetilde{\mathsf{msk}},[\boldsymbol{y}]_2,[v]_2).$ 

Moreover, for all stateful PPT adversaries A, there exists a negligible function negl such that for all  $\lambda \in \mathbb{N}$ , all prmtr =  $(n, \ell, w)$ , we have:

 $\mathsf{adv}^{\mathsf{weak}\mathsf{-SIM}}_{\mathsf{FE},\mathcal{A}}(\lambda,\mathsf{prmtr}) := |\Pr[1 \leftarrow \mathsf{Real}^{\mathsf{FE}}_{\mathcal{A}}(1^{\lambda},\mathsf{prmtr})] - \Pr[1 \leftarrow \mathsf{Ideal}^{\mathsf{FE}}_{\mathcal{A},\mathcal{S}}(1^{\lambda},\mathsf{prmtr})]| = \mathsf{negl}(\lambda),$  where the experiments are defined below.

$$\begin{split} &\frac{\mathsf{Real}^{\mathsf{FE}}_{\mathcal{A}}(1^{\lambda},\mathsf{prmtr}):}{[\boldsymbol{x}]_{1}\leftarrow\mathcal{A}(1^{\lambda})}\\ &\mathsf{pp}\leftarrow\mathsf{PPGen}(1^{\lambda},\mathsf{prmtr})\\ &(\mathsf{pk},\mathsf{msk})\leftarrow\mathsf{Setup}(\mathsf{pp})\\ &\mathsf{ct}\leftarrow\mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{pk},[\boldsymbol{x}]_{1})\\ &\alpha\leftarrow\mathcal{A}^{\mathcal{O}_{\mathsf{KeyGen}}(\cdot)}(\mathsf{ct},\mathsf{pk}) \end{split}$$

$$\begin{array}{c} \overline{\mathsf{Ideal}_{\mathcal{A},\mathcal{S}}^{\mathsf{FE}}(1^{\lambda},\mathsf{prmtr}):} \\ \overline{[\boldsymbol{x}]_1 \leftarrow \mathcal{A}(1^{\lambda})} \\ \mathsf{pp} \leftarrow \mathsf{PPGen}(1^{\lambda},\mathsf{prmtr}) \\ (\widetilde{\mathsf{pk}},\widetilde{\mathsf{msk}}) \leftarrow \widetilde{\mathsf{Setup}}(\mathsf{pp}) \\ \mathsf{ct} \leftarrow \widetilde{\mathsf{Enc}}(\widetilde{\mathsf{msk}}) \\ \alpha \leftarrow \mathcal{A}^{\mathcal{O}_{\mathsf{KeyGen}}(\cdot)}(\mathsf{ct},\widetilde{\mathsf{pk}}) \end{array}$$

In the real experiment, the key generation oracle  $\mathcal{O}_{\mathsf{KeyGen}}$ , when given as input  $[\boldsymbol{y}]_2 \in \mathbf{G}_2^{\dim}$ , returns  $\mathsf{KeyGen}(\mathsf{msk},[\boldsymbol{y}]_2)$ . In the ideal experiment, the key generation oracle  $\mathcal{O}_{\mathsf{KeyGen}}$ , when given as input  $[\boldsymbol{y}]_2 \in \mathbf{G}_2^{\dim}$ , computes  $[\boldsymbol{x}^{\top}\boldsymbol{y}]_2$ , and returns  $\mathsf{KeyGen}_2(\mathsf{msk},[\boldsymbol{y}]_2,[\boldsymbol{x}^{\top}\boldsymbol{y}]_2)$ . Note that this differs from Definition 4.3, where the algorithm  $\mathsf{KeyGen}$  gets as input  $[\boldsymbol{x}^{\top}\boldsymbol{y}]_T \in \mathbf{G}_T$ , not in  $\mathbf{G}_2$ .

{47}------------------------------------------------

### <span id="page-47-0"></span>**8.2 Modular Construction of the Partially-Hiding FE**
