In Fig[.8.2](#page-47-0) we present a modular construction of PHFE for the functionality F PHFE, which relies on an IND-function-hiding FE for the functionality F IPFE and weakly simulationsecure FE for the functionality F IPFE<sup>0</sup> . The simulation security of our PHFE relies on the security of the underlying building blocks and the SXDH assumption in G.

### **Linear efficiency:**

The ciphertexts ct<sup>i</sup> and ct<sup>0</sup> j for all i, j ∈ [n] are encryptions and functional keys associated with vectors of *constant* dimension (in particular, independent of n). Moreover, the order of the group is also independent of n. Thus, by the sheer fact that the encryption and functional key algorithm are polynomial-time, there exists a polynomial p<sup>1</sup> such that the circuit that outputs all the ciphertexts ct<sup>i</sup> and ct<sup>0</sup> j is of size n · p1(λ). By linear efficiency of IPFE, there exists a polynomial p<sup>2</sup> such that the circuit that outputs ct is of size at most n · p2(λ). Overall, the size of the encryption algorithm Enc is n · (p1(λ) + p2(λ)), that is, the scheme has linear efficiency.

### **Correctness:**

By correctness of IPFE [, for all i, j <sup>∈</sup> [n], we have:

$$[\theta_{i,j}]_T = [y_i z_j + rs \boldsymbol{a}_i^{\top} \boldsymbol{b}_j]_T \text{ and } [\theta]_T = f(\boldsymbol{x}, \boldsymbol{y}, \boldsymbol{z}) + rs f(\boldsymbol{x}, \boldsymbol{a}, \boldsymbol{b}),$$

where f(x, a, b) = f <sup>0</sup> Q i∈[`] f i (x)f `+1(a ⊗ b), with a ⊗ b = (a > <sup>i</sup> b<sup>j</sup> )i,j∈[n] ∈ Z n 2 . By correctness of IPFE, for all t ∈ [`], we have:

$$[\boldsymbol{w}_t]_T = \left[ rs \left( f^t(\boldsymbol{u}_t) - f^t(\boldsymbol{x}) \right) \prod_{t < i \le \ell} f^i(\boldsymbol{u}_i) f^{\ell+1}(\boldsymbol{a} \otimes \boldsymbol{b}) \right]_T.$$

Besides, we have:

$$[\theta_{\ell+1}]_T = \left[ rsf^0 \prod_{i \in [\ell]} f^i(\boldsymbol{u}_i) f^{\ell+1}(\boldsymbol{a} \otimes \boldsymbol{b}) \right]_T.$$

Thus, the telescoping sum is of the form:

$$\left[\sum_{t \in [\ell]} f^0 \prod_{0 < i < t} f^i(\boldsymbol{x}) \boldsymbol{w}_t\right]_T = \left[\theta_{\ell+1} - rsf(\boldsymbol{x}, \boldsymbol{a}, \boldsymbol{b})\right]_T.$$

Consequently, we have:

$$[\theta]_T + \left[\sum_{t \in [\ell]} f^0 \prod_{0 < i < t} f^i(\boldsymbol{x}) \boldsymbol{w}_t\right]_T - [\theta_{\ell+1}]_T = [f(\boldsymbol{x}, \boldsymbol{y}, \boldsymbol{z})]_T.$$

{48}------------------------------------------------

### PPGen $(1^{\lambda}, prmtr = (n, w, \ell))$ :

Compute  $\widehat{\mathsf{pp}} \leftarrow \widehat{\mathsf{PPGen}}(1^{\lambda}, \dim = 3)$ ,  $\overline{\mathsf{pp}} \leftarrow \overline{\mathsf{PPGen}}(1^{\lambda}, \dim = n+1)$ . These parameters are both defined relative to the same sequence of groups  $\{\mathcal{G}_{\lambda} = (\mathbf{G}_{1}, \mathbf{G}_{2}, \mathbf{G}_{T}, p, P_{1}, P_{2}, e)\}_{\lambda}$ . Set  $\mathsf{pp} = (\widehat{\mathsf{pp}}, \overline{\mathsf{pp}})$ .

### Setup(pp):

Compute  $(\overline{\mathsf{pk}}, \overline{\mathsf{msk}}) \leftarrow \overline{\mathsf{Setup}}(1^{\lambda}, \dim = n+1)$ . For all  $i, j \in [n]$ :  $\boldsymbol{a}_i, \boldsymbol{b}_j \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathsf{DDH}$ , for all  $k \in [\ell]$ ,  $\boldsymbol{u}_k \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p^n$ . Return  $\mathsf{pk} := (\overline{\mathsf{pk}}, \{[\boldsymbol{a}_i]_1, [\boldsymbol{b}_j]_2\}_{i,j \in [n]})$  and  $\mathsf{msk} := (\overline{\mathsf{msk}}, \{\boldsymbol{a}_i, \boldsymbol{b}_j, \boldsymbol{u}_k\}_{i,j \in [n], k \in [\ell]})$ .

# $\mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{pk}, \boldsymbol{x}, \boldsymbol{y}, \boldsymbol{z} \in (\mathbb{Z}_p)^3)$ :

$$\boxed{ \begin{matrix} r,s \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p, \ (\widehat{\mathsf{pk}},\widehat{\mathsf{msk}}) \end{matrix} \leftarrow \widehat{\mathsf{Setup}}(1^\lambda, \dim = 3), \ \overline{\mathsf{ct}} \leftarrow \overline{\mathsf{Enc}} \left( \overline{\mathsf{pk}}, \binom{rsx}{rs} \right) \end{matrix} . \ \mathsf{For all} \ i,j \in [n]: \\ \mathsf{ct}_i \leftarrow \widehat{\mathsf{Enc}} \left( \widehat{\mathsf{msk}}, \left[ \begin{matrix} y_i \\ a_ir \end{matrix} \right]_1 \right), \mathsf{ct}_j' \leftarrow \widehat{\mathsf{KeyGen}} \left( \widehat{\mathsf{msk}}, \left[ \begin{matrix} z_j \\ b_js \end{matrix} \right]_2 \right) . \ \mathsf{Return} \left( \overline{\mathsf{ct}}, \{\mathsf{ct}_i, \mathsf{ct}_j'\}_{i,j \in [n]} \right). }$$

# $\mathsf{KeyGen}\big(\mathsf{msk},(f^0,\ldots,f^{\ell+1})\big):$

For all  $t \in [\ell]$ , we write  $[\mathbf{M}_t]_2 \in \mathbf{G}_2^{(n+1)\times w}$ , the linear function such that for all  $\begin{bmatrix} \boldsymbol{v} \\ \boldsymbol{\alpha} \end{bmatrix}_1 \in \mathbf{G}_1^{n+1}$ ,  $\begin{bmatrix} \mathbf{M}_t^\top \begin{pmatrix} \boldsymbol{v} \\ \boldsymbol{\alpha} \end{bmatrix} \end{bmatrix}_T = \begin{bmatrix} \left(\alpha \cdot f^t(\boldsymbol{u}_t) - f^t(\boldsymbol{v})\right) \prod_{t < i \leq \ell} f^i(\boldsymbol{u}_i) f^{\ell+1}(\boldsymbol{a} \otimes \boldsymbol{b}) \end{bmatrix}_T \in \mathbf{G}_T^w$ , and  $[\boldsymbol{m}_{\ell+1}]_2 \in \mathbf{G}_2^{n+1}$  the linear function such that for all  $\begin{bmatrix} \boldsymbol{v} \\ \boldsymbol{\alpha} \end{bmatrix}_1 \in \mathbf{G}_1^{n+1}$ ,  $\begin{bmatrix} \boldsymbol{m}_{\ell+1}^\top \begin{pmatrix} \boldsymbol{v} \\ \boldsymbol{\alpha} \end{bmatrix} \end{bmatrix}_T = \begin{bmatrix} \alpha \cdot f^0 \prod_{i \in [\ell]} f^i(\boldsymbol{u}_i) f^{\ell+1}(\boldsymbol{a} \otimes \boldsymbol{b}) \end{bmatrix}_T \in \mathbf{G}_T$ , where  $\boldsymbol{a} \otimes \boldsymbol{b} = (\boldsymbol{a}_i^\top \boldsymbol{b}_j)_{i,j \in [n]} \in \mathbb{Z}^{n^2}$ . For all  $t \in [\ell]$ ,  $\mathsf{sk}_t \leftarrow \overline{\mathsf{KeyGen}}(\overline{\mathsf{msk}}, [\mathbf{M}_t]_2)$ , and  $\mathsf{sk}_{\ell+1} \leftarrow \overline{\mathsf{KeyGen}}(\overline{\mathsf{msk}}, [\boldsymbol{m}_{\ell+1}]_2)$ . Return  $\{\mathsf{sk}_t\}_{t \in [\ell+1]}$ .

### Dec(ct, sk):

Figure 4: This is PHFE, a simulation-secure FE scheme for the functionality  $\mathcal{F}^{phfe}$ . Here,  $\widehat{\mathsf{IPFE}} := (\widehat{\mathsf{Setup}}, \widehat{\mathsf{Enc}}, \widehat{\mathsf{KeyGen}}, \widehat{\mathsf{Dec}})$  is an IND-function-hiding FE for the functionality  $\mathcal{F}^{\mathsf{IPFE}}$ , and  $\widehat{\mathsf{IPFE}} := (\widehat{\mathsf{Setup}}, \widehat{\mathsf{Enc}}, \widehat{\mathsf{KeyGen}}, \widehat{\mathsf{Dec}})$  is a weakly simulation-secure FE for the functionality  $\mathcal{F}^{\mathsf{IPFE}}$ .

{49}------------------------------------------------

<span id="page-49-0"></span>**Theorem 8.1** (Simulation security). The scheme presented in Fig. 8.2 is simulation secure (as per Definition 4.3), provided the underlying  $\widehat{\mathsf{IPFE}}$  is indistinguishability function-hiding secure (as defined in Definition 4.4), and  $\widehat{\mathsf{IPFE}}$  is simulation secure as per Definition 8.1, which is implied by the notion given in Definition 4.3. Namely, for any PPT adversary  $\mathcal{A}$ , there exist PPT adversaries  $\mathcal{B}_1$ ,  $\mathcal{B}_2$ ,  $\mathcal{B}_3$  and  $\mathcal{B}_4$  such that:

$$\mathsf{adv}^{\mathsf{SIM}}_{\mathsf{PHFE},\mathcal{A}}(\lambda) \leq \mathsf{adv}^{\mathsf{weak-SIM}}_{\widehat{\mathsf{IPFE}},\mathcal{B}_1}(\lambda) + (\ell+1) \cdot \mathsf{adv}^{\mathsf{DDH}}_{\mathbf{G}_2,\mathcal{B}_2}(\lambda) + 3 \cdot \mathsf{adv}^{\mathsf{DDH}}_{\mathbf{G}_1,\mathcal{B}_3}(\lambda) + \mathsf{adv}^{\mathsf{IND-FH}}_{\widehat{\mathsf{IPFE}},\mathcal{B}_4}(\lambda) + \frac{2}{p}.$$

*Proof.* The proof proceeds using a series of hybrid games, described below. Let  $\mathcal{A}$  be a PPT adversary against the simulation security of the scheme. For any game  $\mathbf{Hybrid}_i$ , we denote by  $\mathsf{adv}_i := \Pr[1 \leftarrow \mathbf{Hybrid}_i(\mathcal{A})]$  the probability that  $\mathbf{Hybrid}_i$  returns 1 when interacting with  $\mathcal{A}$ .

- $\mathbf{Hybrid}_0$ : is the real experiment as given in Definition 4.3.
- $\underline{\mathbf{Hybrid}_1}$ : is the same as  $\underline{\mathbf{Hybrid}_0}$ , except we replace the scheme ( $\overline{\mathsf{Setup}}$ ,  $\overline{\mathsf{Enc}}$ ,  $\overline{\mathsf{KeyGen}}$ ) by its  $\underline{\mathsf{simulator}}$  ( $\underbrace{\mathsf{Setup}}$ ,  $\underbrace{\mathsf{Enc}}$ ,  $\underbrace{\mathsf{KeyGen}_2}$ ). That is, we sample ( $\widehat{\mathsf{pk}}$ ,  $\widehat{\mathsf{msk}}$ )  $\leftarrow$   $\underbrace{\mathsf{Setup}}$ ( $\overline{\mathsf{pp}}$ ), instead of ( $\overline{\mathsf{pk}}$ ,  $\overline{\mathsf{msk}}$ )  $\leftarrow$   $\underbrace{\mathsf{Setup}}$ ( $\overline{\mathsf{pp}}$ ).

The challenge ciphertext is generated using  $\overline{\operatorname{ct}} \leftarrow \widetilde{\operatorname{Enc}}\left(\widetilde{\operatorname{msk}}\right)$  instead of  $\overline{\operatorname{ct}} \leftarrow \overline{\operatorname{Enc}}\left(\overline{\operatorname{pk}}, \binom{rs\boldsymbol{x}}{rs}\right)$ . The functional secret keys are generated using, for all  $t \in [\ell]$ :

$$\mathsf{sk}_t \leftarrow \widetilde{\mathsf{KeyGen}}_2 \left( \widetilde{\mathsf{msk}}, \left[ \mathbf{M}_t \right]_2, \left[ rs \big( f^t(\boldsymbol{u}_t) - f^t(\boldsymbol{x}) \big) \prod_{t < i \leq \ell} f^i(\boldsymbol{u}_i) f^{\ell+1}(\boldsymbol{a} \otimes \boldsymbol{b}), \right]_2 \right)$$

and

$$\mathsf{sk}_{\ell+1} \leftarrow \widetilde{\mathsf{KeyGen}}_2 \left( \widetilde{\mathsf{msk}}, [\boldsymbol{m}_{\ell+1}]_2, \left\lceil rsf^0 \prod_{i \in [\ell]} f^i(\boldsymbol{u}_i) f^{\ell+1}(\boldsymbol{a} \otimes \boldsymbol{b}) \right\rceil_2 \right),$$

where  $\boldsymbol{a}\otimes \boldsymbol{b}=(\boldsymbol{a}_i^{\top}\boldsymbol{b}_j)_{i,j\in[n]}\in\mathbb{Z}^{n^2}.$ 

This transition is justified by the simulation security of  $\overline{\mathsf{IPFE}}$ . Namely, there is a PPT adversary  $\mathcal{B}_0$  such that:

$$|\mathsf{adv}_0 - \mathsf{adv}_1| \leq \mathsf{adv}_{\overline{\mathsf{IPFE}},\mathcal{B}_0}^{\mathsf{weak-SIM}}(\lambda).$$

•  $\underline{\mathbf{Hybrid}}_2$ : is the same as  $\underline{\mathbf{Hybrid}}_1$ , except we replace the vectors  $\{u_k\}_{k\in[\ell]}$  by  $\{u_k+x\}_{k\in[\ell]}$ . These values are identically distributed, since the vectors  $u_k$  are sampled uniformly over  $\mathbb{Z}_p^n$ , independently of the challenge x, which is chosen beforehand. Consequently, the functional secret keys are now generated using, for all  $t \in [\ell]$ :

$$\mathsf{sk}_t \leftarrow \widetilde{\mathsf{KeyGen}}_2 \left( \widetilde{\mathsf{msk}}, \left[ \mathbf{M}_t \right]_2, \left[ rsf^t(\boldsymbol{u}_t) \prod_{t < i \leq \ell} f^i(\boldsymbol{u}_i + \boldsymbol{x}) f^{\ell+1}(\boldsymbol{a} \otimes \boldsymbol{b}), \right]_2 \right)$$

and

$$\mathsf{sk}_{\ell+1} \leftarrow \widetilde{\mathsf{KeyGen}}_2 \left( \widetilde{\mathsf{msk}}, [\boldsymbol{m}_{\ell+1}]_2, \left[ rsf^0 \prod_{i \in [\ell]} f^i(\boldsymbol{u}_i + \boldsymbol{x}) f^{\ell+1}(\boldsymbol{a} \otimes \boldsymbol{b}) \right]_2 \right).$$

{50}------------------------------------------------

Here, we use the fact that the functions  $f^i$  for all i > 0 are linear. We have:

$$adv_1 = adv_2$$
.

• Hybrid<sub>3</sub>: is the same as Hybrid<sub>2</sub>, except we replace the vectors  $[su_k]_2$  by fresh  $[s_k]_2 \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} G_2^{\overline{n}}$  for all  $k \in [\ell]$ , using the DDH assumption in  $G_2$ . Consequently, the functional secret keys are now generated using, for all  $t \in [\ell]$ :

$$\mathsf{sk}_t \leftarrow \widetilde{\mathsf{KeyGen}}_2 \left( \widetilde{\mathsf{msk}}, \left[ \mathbf{M}_t \right]_2, \left[ rf^t(\boldsymbol{s}_t) \prod_{t < i \leq \ell} f^i(\boldsymbol{u}_i + \boldsymbol{x}) f^{\ell+1}(\boldsymbol{a} \otimes \boldsymbol{b}), \right]_2 \right)$$

and

$$\mathsf{sk}_{\ell+1} \leftarrow \widetilde{\mathsf{KeyGen}}_2\left(\widetilde{\mathsf{msk}}, [\boldsymbol{m}_{\ell+1}]_2, [v]_2\right),$$

where

$$[v]_2 = \left[ rsf(\boldsymbol{x}, \boldsymbol{a}, \boldsymbol{b}) + r \sum_{i \in [\ell]} \left( \prod_{j < i} f^j(\boldsymbol{x}) \right) f^i(\boldsymbol{s}_i) \left( \prod_{j > i} f^j(\boldsymbol{u}_j + \boldsymbol{x}) \right) f^{\ell+1}(\boldsymbol{a} \otimes \boldsymbol{b}) \right]_2.$$

We proceed via a hybrid argument, switching the vector  $[s\boldsymbol{u}_k]_2$  to uniformly random  $[s_k]_2 \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p^n$  one index  $k \in [\ell]$  at a time. That is, we define  $\mathbf{Hybrid}_{2,\rho}$  for all  $\rho \in [0,d]$  as  $\mathbf{Hybrid}_2$ , except the first  $\rho$ -th functional keys are computed as in  $\mathbf{Hybrid}_3$ . For all  $\rho \in [\ell]$ , we show there exists a PPT adversary  $\mathcal{B}_{2,\rho}$  such that  $|\mathsf{adv}_{2,\rho-1} - \mathsf{adv}_{2,\rho}| \leq \mathsf{adv}_{\mathbf{G}_2,\mathcal{B}_{2,\rho}}^{\mathsf{DDH}}(\lambda)$ .

The adversary  $\mathcal{B}_{2.\rho}$  takes as input a tuple  $([s]_2, [\boldsymbol{u}_\rho]_2, [s_\rho]_2)$  where the value  $[s_\rho]_2$  is either of the form  $[s\boldsymbol{u}_\rho]_2$  (case 1), or uniformly random over  $\mathbf{G}_2^n$  (case 2). The adversary  $\mathcal{B}_{2.\rho}$  samples  $r \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p$ ,  $\boldsymbol{a}_i, \boldsymbol{b}_j \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}}$  DDH for all  $i, j \in [n]$ ,  $\boldsymbol{u}_m \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p^n$  for all  $m \neq \rho$ ,  $\boldsymbol{s}_t \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p^n$  for all  $t < \rho$ ,  $(\widetilde{\mathsf{msk}}, \widetilde{\mathsf{pk}}) \leftarrow \widetilde{\mathsf{Setup}}(\overline{\mathsf{pp}})$ , upon which it can simulate the view of the adversary  $\mathcal{A}$ . In case 1,  $\mathcal{B}_{2.\rho}$  simulates  $\mathsf{Hybrid}_{2.\rho-1}$  to  $\mathcal{A}$ , whereas it simulates  $\mathsf{Hybrid}_{2.\rho}$  in case 2.

Putting everything together, we have the existence of a PPT adversary  $\mathcal{B}_2$  such that:

$$|\mathsf{adv}_2 - \mathsf{adv}_3| \leq \ell \cdot \mathsf{adv}_{\mathbf{G}_2, \mathcal{B}_2}^{\mathsf{DDH}}(\lambda)$$

• <u>Hybrid</u><sub>4</sub>: is the same as Hybrid<sub>3</sub>, except that we replace the values  $[\boldsymbol{b}_j s]_2$  used for generating functional secret keys by fresh  $[\boldsymbol{w}_j]_2 \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbf{G}_2^2$  for all  $j \in [n]$ , using the DDH assumption in  $\mathbf{G}_2$ .

Consequently, the challenge ciphertext now contains:

$$\mathsf{ct}_j' \leftarrow \widehat{\mathsf{KeyGen}} \left( \widehat{\mathsf{msk}}, \begin{bmatrix} z_j \\ -\boldsymbol{w}_j \end{bmatrix}_2 \right).$$

Moreover, the functional secret keys are now generated using:

$$\mathsf{sk}_{\ell+1} \leftarrow \widetilde{\mathsf{KeyGen}}_2\left(\widetilde{\mathsf{msk}}, [\boldsymbol{m}_{\ell+1}]_2, [v]_2\right),$$

{51}------------------------------------------------

where

$$[v]_2 = \left[ rf(\boldsymbol{x}, \boldsymbol{a}, \boldsymbol{w}) + r \sum_{i \in [\ell]} \left( \prod_{j < i} f^j(\boldsymbol{x}) \right) f^i(\boldsymbol{s}_i) \left( \prod_{j > i} f^j(\boldsymbol{u}_j + \boldsymbol{x}) \right) f^{\ell+1}(\boldsymbol{a} \otimes \boldsymbol{b}) \right]_2.$$

We show there exists a PPT adversary  $\mathcal{B}_3$  such that:

$$|\mathsf{adv}_3 - \mathsf{adv}_4| \leq \mathsf{adv}^\mathsf{DDH}_{\mathbf{G}_2,\mathcal{B}_3}(\lambda).$$

The adversary  $\mathcal{B}_1$  takes as input a tuple  $([s]_2, \{[b_j]_2, [w_j]_2\}_{j \in [n]})$  where the values  $[w_j]_2$  are either of the form  $[b_j s]_2$  (case 1), or uniformly random over  $\mathbf{G}_2^2$  (case 2). The adversary  $\mathcal{B}_3$  samples  $r \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p$ ,  $(\widetilde{\mathsf{msk}}, \widetilde{\mathsf{pk}}) \leftarrow \widetilde{\mathsf{Setup}}(\overline{\mathsf{pp}})$ ,  $a_i \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathsf{DDH}$  for all  $i \in [n]$ ,  $u_k, s_k \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p^n$  for all  $k \in [\ell]$ , upon which it can simulate the view of the adversary  $\mathcal{A}$  straightforwardly. In case 1, it simulates  $\mathsf{Hybrid}_3$  to  $\mathcal{A}$ , whereas it simulates  $\mathsf{Hybrid}_4$  in case 2.

•  $\underline{\mathbf{Hybrid}_5}$ : is the same as  $\mathbf{Hybrid}_4$ , except we use the key generation algorithm  $\widecheck{\mathsf{KeyGen}_1}$ , which takes inputs from  $\mathbf{G}_1$  instead of  $\widecheck{\mathsf{KeyGen}_2}$ , which takes inputs from  $\mathbf{G}_2$ . Namely, the secret keys are now generated using, for all  $t \in [\ell]$ :

$$\mathsf{sk}_t \leftarrow \widetilde{\mathsf{KeyGen}}_1 \left( \widetilde{\mathsf{msk}}, \left[ \mathbf{M}_t \right]_1, \left[ rf^t(\boldsymbol{s}_t) \prod_{t < i \leq \ell} f^i(\boldsymbol{u}_i + \boldsymbol{x}) f^{\ell+1}(\boldsymbol{a} \otimes \boldsymbol{b}), \right]_1 \right)$$

and

$$\mathsf{sk}_{\ell+1} \leftarrow \widetilde{\mathsf{KeyGen}}_1 \left( \widetilde{\mathsf{msk}}, [\boldsymbol{m}_{\ell+1}]_1, [v]_1 \right),$$

where

$$[v]_1 = \left[ rf(\boldsymbol{x}, \boldsymbol{a}, \boldsymbol{w}) + r \sum_{i \in [\ell]} \left( \prod_{j < i} f^j(\boldsymbol{x}) \right) f^i(\boldsymbol{s}_i) \left( \prod_{j > i} f^j(\boldsymbol{u}_j + \boldsymbol{x}) \right) f^{\ell+1}(\boldsymbol{a} \otimes \boldsymbol{b}) \right]_1.$$

By definition of the weak simulation security (cf Definition 8.1), the output of  $\widetilde{\text{KeyGen}}_1$  and  $\widetilde{\text{KeyGen}}_2$  are identically distributed, thus:

$$\mathsf{adv}_4 = \mathsf{adv}_5.$$

•  $\underline{\mathbf{Hybrid}_6}$ : is the same as  $\underline{\mathbf{Hybrid}_5}$ , except that we replace the values  $[a_ir]_1$  by fresh  $[v_i]_1 \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbf{G}_1^2$  for all  $i \in [n]$ , using the DDH assumption in  $\mathbf{G}_1$ . Consequently, the challenge ciphertext now contains:

$$\mathsf{ct}_i \leftarrow \widehat{\mathsf{KeyGen}}\left(\widehat{\mathsf{msk}}, \begin{bmatrix} y_i \\ \bm{v}_i \end{bmatrix}_1 \right).$$

{52}------------------------------------------------

Moreover, the secret keys are now generated using, for all  $t \in [\ell]$ :

$$\mathsf{sk}_t \leftarrow \widetilde{\mathsf{KeyGen}}_1 \left( \widetilde{\mathsf{msk}}, \left[ \mathbf{M}_t \right]_1, \left[ f^t(\boldsymbol{s}_t) \prod_{t < i \leq \ell} f^i(\boldsymbol{u}_i + \boldsymbol{x}) f^{\ell+1}(\boldsymbol{v} \otimes \boldsymbol{b}), \right]_1 \right)$$

and

$$\mathsf{sk}_{\ell+1} \leftarrow \widetilde{\mathsf{KeyGen}}_1 \left( \widetilde{\mathsf{msk}}, [\boldsymbol{m}_{\ell+1}]_1, [v]_1 \right),$$

where

$$[v]_1 = \left[ f(\boldsymbol{x}, \boldsymbol{v}, \boldsymbol{w}) + \sum_{i \in [\ell]} \Big( \prod_{j < i} f^j(\boldsymbol{x}) \Big) f^i(\boldsymbol{s}_i) \Big( \prod_{j > i} f^j(\boldsymbol{u}_j + \boldsymbol{x}) \Big) f^{\ell+1}(\boldsymbol{v} \otimes \boldsymbol{b}) \right]_1.$$

We show there exists a PPT adversary  $\mathcal{B}_5$  such that:

$$|\mathsf{adv}_5 - \mathsf{adv}_6| \leq \mathsf{adv}_{\mathbf{G}_1,\mathcal{B}_5}^\mathsf{DDH}(\lambda)$$

The adversary  $\mathcal{B}_5$  takes as input a tuple  $\left([r]_1, \{[a_i]_1, [v_i]_1\}_{i \in [n]}\right)$  where the values  $[v_i]_1$  are either of the form  $[a_i r]_1$  (case 1), or uniformly random over  $\mathbf{G}_1^2$  (case 2). The adversary  $\mathcal{B}_5$  samples  $(\widetilde{\mathsf{msk}}, \widetilde{\mathsf{pk}}) \leftarrow \widetilde{\mathsf{Setup}}(\overline{\mathsf{pp}})$ ,  $\boldsymbol{b}_j \leftarrow_{\mathsf{R}} \mathsf{DDH}$ ,  $\boldsymbol{w}_j \leftarrow_{\mathsf{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p^2$  for all  $j \in [n]$ ,  $\boldsymbol{u}_k, \boldsymbol{s}_k \leftarrow_{\mathsf{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p^n$  for all  $k \in [\ell]$ , upon which it can simulate the view of the adversary  $\mathcal{A}$  straightforwardly. In case 1, it simulates  $\mathsf{Hybrid}_5$  to  $\mathcal{A}$ , whereas it simulates  $\mathsf{Hybrid}_6$  in case 2.

• <u>Hybrid</u><sub>7</sub>: is the same as Hybrid<sub>6</sub>, except we replace the values  $\{v_i\}_{i\in[n]}$  by  $\{v_i+y_ih\}_{i\in[n]}$ , where  $h \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p^2$ . These values are identically distributed, since the  $v_i$  are sampled uniformly over  $\mathbb{Z}_p^2$ , independently of the challenge  $\{y_i\}_{i\in[n]}$ , which is chosen beforehand. Therefore, we have:

$$adv_6 = adv_7$$
.

Consequently, the challenge ciphertext now contains:

$$\mathsf{ct}_i \leftarrow \widehat{\mathsf{KeyGen}}\left(\widehat{\mathsf{msk}}, \begin{bmatrix} y_i \\ \boldsymbol{v}_i + y_i \boldsymbol{h} \end{bmatrix}_1 \right).$$

Moreover, the secret keys are now generated using for all  $t \in [\ell]$ :

$$\mathsf{sk}_t \leftarrow \widetilde{\mathsf{KeyGen}}_1 \left( \widetilde{\mathsf{msk}}, \left[ \mathbf{M}_t \right]_1, \left[ f^t(\boldsymbol{s}_t) \prod_{t < i \leq \ell} f^i(\boldsymbol{u}_i + \boldsymbol{x}) f^{\ell+1}((\boldsymbol{v} + \boldsymbol{y} \otimes \boldsymbol{h}) \otimes \boldsymbol{b}), \right]_1 \right),$$

and

$$\mathsf{sk}_{\ell+1} \leftarrow \widetilde{\mathsf{KeyGen}}_1 \left( \widetilde{\mathsf{msk}}, [\boldsymbol{m}_{\ell+1}]_1, [v]_1 \right),$$

where

$$[v]_1 = \left[ f(\boldsymbol{x}, \boldsymbol{v} + \boldsymbol{y} \otimes \boldsymbol{h}, \boldsymbol{w}) + \sum_{i \in [\ell]} \left( \prod_{j < i} f^j(\boldsymbol{x}) \right) f^i(\boldsymbol{s}_i) \left( \prod_{j > i} f^j(\boldsymbol{u}_j + \boldsymbol{x}) \right) f^{\ell+1}(\boldsymbol{v} + \boldsymbol{y} \otimes \boldsymbol{h}) \otimes \boldsymbol{b} \right) \right]_1$$

{53}------------------------------------------------

where  $\boldsymbol{y}\otimes\boldsymbol{h}=(y_j\cdot\boldsymbol{h})_{j\in[n]}\in\mathbb{Z}^{2n}$ , and  $(\boldsymbol{v}+\boldsymbol{y}\otimes\boldsymbol{h})\otimes\boldsymbol{b}=((\boldsymbol{v}_i+y_i\boldsymbol{h})^{\top}\boldsymbol{b}_j)_{i,j\in[n]}\in\mathbb{Z}^{n^2}$ .

• Hybrid<sub>8</sub>: is the same as Hybrid<sub>7</sub>, except that we replace the values  $[\boldsymbol{v}_i + y_i \boldsymbol{h}]_1$  by  $[\boldsymbol{d}\overline{r_i + y_i \boldsymbol{h}}]_1$  with  $\boldsymbol{d} \leftarrow \text{DDH}$  and  $r_i \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p$  for all  $i \in [n]$ , using the DDH assumption in  $\mathbf{G}_1$ . Consequently, the ciphertexts now contains:

$$\mathsf{ct}_i \leftarrow \widehat{\mathsf{KeyGen}}\left(\widehat{\mathsf{msk}}, \begin{bmatrix} y_i \\ dr_i + y_i h \end{bmatrix}_1\right).$$

Moreover, the secret keys are now generated using for all  $t \in [\ell]$ :

$$\mathsf{sk}_t \leftarrow \widetilde{\mathsf{KeyGen}}_1 \left( \widetilde{\mathsf{msk}}, \left[ \mathbf{M}_t \right]_1, \left[ f^t(\boldsymbol{s}_t) \prod_{t < i \leq \ell} f^i(\boldsymbol{u}_i + \boldsymbol{x}) f^{\ell+1}((\boldsymbol{r} \otimes \boldsymbol{d} + \boldsymbol{y} \otimes \boldsymbol{h}) \otimes \boldsymbol{b}), \right]_1 \right),$$

and

$$\mathsf{sk}_{\ell+1} \leftarrow \widetilde{\mathsf{KeyGen}}_1 \left( \widetilde{\mathsf{msk}}, [\bm{m}_{\ell+1}]_1, [v]_1 \right),$$

where

$$[v]_1 = \left[ f(\boldsymbol{x}, \boldsymbol{r} \otimes \boldsymbol{d} + \boldsymbol{y} \otimes \boldsymbol{h}, \boldsymbol{w}) + \sum_{i \in [\ell]} \left( \prod_{j < i} f^j(\boldsymbol{x}) \right) f^i(\boldsymbol{s}_i) \left( \prod_{j > i} f^j(\boldsymbol{u}_j + \boldsymbol{x}) \right) f^{\ell+1}(\boldsymbol{r} \otimes \boldsymbol{d} + \boldsymbol{y} \otimes \boldsymbol{h}) \otimes \boldsymbol{b} \right) \right]_1,$$

where  $\mathbf{r} \otimes \mathbf{d} = (r_i \cdot \mathbf{d})_{i \in [n]} \in \mathbb{Z}^{2n}$ , and  $(\mathbf{r} \otimes \mathbf{d} + \mathbf{y} \otimes \mathbf{h}) \otimes \mathbf{b} = ((\mathbf{d}r_i + y_i\mathbf{v})^{\top}\mathbf{b}_j)_{i,j \in [n]} \in \mathbb{Z}^{n^2}$ . We show there exists a PPT adversary  $\mathcal{B}_7$  such that:

$$|\mathsf{adv}_7 - \mathsf{adv}_8| \leq \mathsf{adv}_{\mathbf{G}_1,\mathcal{B}_7}^\mathsf{DDH}(\lambda).$$

The adversary  $\mathcal{B}_7$  takes as input a tuple  $([\boldsymbol{d}]_1, \{[\boldsymbol{v}_i]_1\}_{i\in[n]})$  where the values  $[\boldsymbol{v}_i]_1$  are either of the form  $[\boldsymbol{d}r_i]_1$  (case 1), or uniformly random over  $\mathbf{G}_1^2$  (case 2). The adversary  $\mathcal{B}_7$  samples  $\boldsymbol{h} \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p^2$ ,  $(\widetilde{\mathsf{msk}}, \widetilde{\mathsf{pk}}) \leftarrow \widetilde{\mathsf{Setup}}(\overline{\mathsf{pp}})$ ,  $\boldsymbol{a}_i, \boldsymbol{b}_j \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathsf{DDH}$ ,  $\boldsymbol{w}_j \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p^2$  for all  $i, j \in [n]$ ,  $\boldsymbol{u}_k, \boldsymbol{s}_k \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p^n$  for all  $k \in [\ell]$ , upon which it can simulate the view of the adversary  $\mathcal{A}$  straightforwardly. In case 1, it simulates  $\mathsf{Hybrid}_8$  to  $\mathcal{A}$ , whereas it simulates  $\mathsf{Hybrid}_7$  in case 2.

• <u>Hybrid</u><sub>9</sub>: is the same as Hybrid<sub>8</sub>, except 1) we change the distribution of  $\boldsymbol{h}$  from uniformly random over  $\mathbb{Z}_p^2$  to uniformly random over  $\mathbb{Z}_p^2 \setminus \operatorname{Span}(\boldsymbol{d})$ , which only induces a statistical change of 1/p, given  $\operatorname{Span}(\boldsymbol{d})$  is of size at most p; 2) we replace the values  $\{\boldsymbol{w}_j\}_{j\in[n]}$  by  $\{\boldsymbol{w}_j+z_i\boldsymbol{d}^\perp\}_{j\in[n]}$ , where  $\boldsymbol{d}^\perp\in\mathbb{Z}_p^2$  is such that  $\boldsymbol{d}^\top\boldsymbol{d}^\perp=0$  and  $\boldsymbol{h}^\top\boldsymbol{d}^\perp=1$  (note that such a vector exists as long as  $\boldsymbol{h}\notin\operatorname{Span}(\boldsymbol{d})$ ). These values are identically distributed, since the  $\boldsymbol{w}_j$  are sampled uniformly over  $\mathbb{Z}_p^2$ , independently of the challenge  $\{z_j\}_{j\in[n]}$ , which is chosen beforehand. Therefore, we have:

$$|\mathsf{adv}_8 - \mathsf{adv}_9| \leq \frac{1}{p}.$$

Consequently, the ciphertexts now contains:

$$\mathsf{ct}_j' \leftarrow \widehat{\mathsf{KeyGen}}\left(\widehat{\mathsf{msk}}, \begin{bmatrix} z_j \ -\boldsymbol{w}_j - z_j \boldsymbol{d}^\perp \end{bmatrix}_1\right).$$

{54}------------------------------------------------

Moreover, the secret keys are now generated using:

$$\mathsf{sk}_{\ell+1} \leftarrow \widetilde{\mathsf{KeyGen}}_1 \left( \widetilde{\mathsf{msk}}, [\boldsymbol{m}_{\ell+1}]_1, [v]_1 \right),$$

where

$$[v]_1 = \left[ f(\boldsymbol{x}, \boldsymbol{r} \otimes \boldsymbol{d} + \boldsymbol{y} \otimes \boldsymbol{h}, \boldsymbol{w}) + f(\boldsymbol{x}, \boldsymbol{y}, \boldsymbol{z}) + \sum_{i \in [\ell]} \left( \prod_{j < i} f^j(\boldsymbol{x}) \right) f^i(\boldsymbol{s}_i) \left( \prod_{j > i} f^j(\boldsymbol{u}_j + \boldsymbol{x}) \right) f^{\ell+1} \left( (\boldsymbol{r} \otimes \boldsymbol{d} + \boldsymbol{y} \otimes \boldsymbol{h}) \otimes \boldsymbol{b}) \right) \right]_1.$$

•  $\mathbf{Hybrid}_{10}$ : is the same as  $\mathbf{Hybrid}_{9}$ , except the challenge ciphertext contains:

$$\mathsf{ct}_i \leftarrow \widehat{\mathsf{Enc}}\left(\widehat{\mathsf{msk}}, \begin{bmatrix} 0 \\ \mathbf{d}r_i + y_i \mathbf{h} \end{bmatrix}_1\right), \ \mathsf{ct}_j' \leftarrow \widehat{\mathsf{KeyGen}}\left(\widehat{\mathsf{msk}}, \begin{bmatrix} 0 \\ -\mathbf{w}_j \end{bmatrix}_1\right)$$

instead of

$$\mathsf{ct}_i \leftarrow \widehat{\mathsf{Enc}}\left(\widehat{\mathsf{msk}}, \begin{bmatrix} y_i \\ \boldsymbol{d}r_i + y_i \boldsymbol{h} \end{bmatrix}_1\right), \ \mathsf{ct}_j' \leftarrow \widehat{\mathsf{KeyGen}}\left(\widehat{\mathsf{msk}}, \begin{bmatrix} z_j \\ -\boldsymbol{w}_j - z_j \boldsymbol{d}^\perp \end{bmatrix}_1\right).$$

This transition is justified by the function-hiding IND security of  $\widehat{\mathsf{IPFE}}$ , which can be used since for all  $i, j \in [n]$ , we have  $\begin{pmatrix} y_i \\ dr_i + y_i h \end{pmatrix}^\top \begin{pmatrix} z_j \\ -w_j - z_j d^\perp \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} 0 \\ dr_i + y_i h \end{pmatrix}^\top \begin{pmatrix} 0 \\ -w_j \end{pmatrix}$ . The equality uses the fact that  $d^\top d^\perp = 0$  and  $h^\top d^\perp = 1$ .

There exists a PPT adversary  $\mathcal{B}_9$  such that:

$$|\mathsf{adv}_9 - \mathsf{adv}_{10}| \leq \mathsf{adv}_{\widehat{\mathsf{IPFE}},\mathcal{B}_9}^{\mathsf{IND-FH}}(\lambda).$$

The adversary  $\mathcal{B}_9$  first samples  $\boldsymbol{d} \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathsf{DDH}$ ,  $\boldsymbol{h} \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p^2 \backslash \mathsf{Span}(\boldsymbol{d})$ ,  $\boldsymbol{d}^\perp \in \mathbb{Z}_p^2$  such that  $\boldsymbol{d}^\top \boldsymbol{d}^\perp = 0$  and  $\boldsymbol{h}^\top \boldsymbol{d}^\perp = 1$ ,  $(\widetilde{\mathsf{msk}}, \widetilde{\mathsf{pk}}) \leftarrow \widetilde{\mathsf{Setup}}(\overline{\mathsf{pp}})$ ,  $r_i \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p$ ,  $\boldsymbol{a}_i, \boldsymbol{b}_j \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathsf{DDH}$ ,  $\boldsymbol{w}_j \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p^2$  for all  $i, j \in [n]$ ,  $\boldsymbol{u}_k, \boldsymbol{s}_k \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p^n$  for all  $k \in [\ell]$ . It sends the challenge

$$\left\{ \begin{bmatrix} y_i \\ \boldsymbol{d}r_i + y_i \boldsymbol{h} \end{bmatrix}_1, \begin{bmatrix} 0 \\ \boldsymbol{d}r_i + y_i \boldsymbol{h} \end{bmatrix}_1 \right\}_{i \in [n]}, \left\{ \begin{bmatrix} z_j \\ -\boldsymbol{w}_j - z_j \boldsymbol{d}^{\perp} \end{bmatrix}_1, \begin{bmatrix} 0 \\ -\boldsymbol{w}_j \end{bmatrix}_1 \right\}_{j \in [n]}$$

to its own experiment, upon which it receives  $\{ct_i\}_{i\in[n]}$ , encryptions of the left or right challenges; together with  $\{ct_j'\}_{j\in[n]}$ , functional secret keys associated with the left or right challenges. In the left case,  $\mathcal{B}_9$  simulates  $\mathbf{Hybrid}_9$  to  $\mathcal{A}$ , whereas it simulates  $\mathbf{Hybrid}_{10}$  in the right case.

• <u>Hybrid</u><sub>11</sub>: is the same as Hybrid<sub>10</sub>, except 1) we change the distribution of h from uniformly random over  $\mathbb{Z}_p^2 \setminus \text{Span}(d)$  to uniformly random over  $\mathbb{Z}_p^2$ ; this introduces a statistical distance of 1/p since the size of Span(d) is at most p; 2) we replace the values  $\{[dr_i + y_ih]_1\}_{i \in [n]}$  by  $\{[v_i + y_ih]_1\}_{i \in [n]}$ , where  $v_i \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p^2$  for all  $i \in [n]$ , using the DDH assumption in  $G_1$ . This transition is the reverse to the transition from Hybrid<sub>5</sub> to Hybrid<sub>6</sub>.

{55}------------------------------------------------

Consequently, the challenge ciphertext now contains:

$$\mathsf{ct}_i \leftarrow \widehat{\mathsf{Enc}}\left(\widehat{\mathsf{msk}}, \begin{bmatrix} 0 \\ \pmb{v}_i + y_i \pmb{h} \end{bmatrix}_1\right),$$

and the secret keys are now generated using, for all  $t \in [\ell]$ :

$$\mathsf{sk}_t \leftarrow \widetilde{\mathsf{KeyGen}}_1 \left( \widetilde{\mathsf{msk}}, \left[ \mathbf{M}_t \right]_1, \left[ f^t(\boldsymbol{s}_t) \prod_{t < i \leq \ell} f^i(\boldsymbol{u}_i + \boldsymbol{x}) f^{\ell+1}((\boldsymbol{v} + \boldsymbol{y} \otimes \boldsymbol{h}) \otimes \boldsymbol{b}), \right]_1 \right),$$

and

$$\mathsf{sk}_{\ell+1} \leftarrow \widetilde{\mathsf{KeyGen}}_1 \left( \widetilde{\mathsf{msk}}, [\bm{m}_{\ell+1}]_1, [v]_1 \right),$$

where

$$[v]_1 = \left[ f(\boldsymbol{x}, \boldsymbol{v} + \boldsymbol{y} \otimes \boldsymbol{h}, \boldsymbol{w}) + f(\boldsymbol{x}, \boldsymbol{y}, \boldsymbol{z}) + \sum_{i \in [\ell]} \left( \prod_{j < i} f^j(\boldsymbol{x}) \right) f^i(\boldsymbol{s}_i) \left( \prod_{j > i} f^j(\boldsymbol{u}_j + \boldsymbol{x}) \right) f^{\ell+1} \left( (\boldsymbol{v} + \boldsymbol{y} \otimes \boldsymbol{h}) \otimes \boldsymbol{b} \right) \right]_1.$$

We show there exists a PPT adversary  $\mathcal{B}_{10}$  such that:

$$|\mathsf{adv}_{10} - \mathsf{adv}_{11}| \leq \mathsf{adv}^{\mathsf{DDH}}_{\mathbf{G}_1,\mathcal{B}_{10}}(\lambda) + \frac{1}{p}.$$

The adversary  $\mathcal{B}_{10}$  takes as input a tuple  $([d]_1, \{[v_i]_1\}_{i \in [n]})$  where the vectors  $[v_i]_1$  are either of the form  $[dr_i]_1$  (case 1), or uniformly random over  $\mathbf{G}_1^2$  (case 2). The adversary  $\mathcal{B}_{10}$  samples  $\mathbf{h} \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p^2$ ,  $(\widetilde{\mathsf{msk}}, \widetilde{\mathsf{pk}}) \leftarrow \widetilde{\mathsf{Setup}}(\overline{\mathsf{pp}})$ ,  $\mathbf{a}_i, \mathbf{b}_j \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathsf{DDH}$ ,  $\mathbf{w}_j \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p^2$  for all  $i, j \in [n]$ ,  $\mathbf{u}_k, \mathbf{s}_k \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p^n$  and for all  $k \in [\ell]$ , upon which it can simulate the view of the adversary  $\mathcal{A}$  straightforwardly. In case 1, it simulates  $\mathbf{Hybrid}_{11}$  to  $\mathcal{A}$ , whereas it simulates  $\mathbf{Hybrid}_{10}$  in case 2.

•  $\underline{\mathbf{Hybrid}_{12}}$ : is the same as  $\underline{\mathbf{Hybrid}_{11}}$ , except we replace the values  $\{v_i + y_i h\}_{i \in [n]}$  by  $\{v_i\}_{i \in [n]}$ . These values are identically distributed, since the  $v_i$  are sampled uniformly over  $\mathbb{Z}_p^2$ , independently of the challenge  $\{y_i\}_{i \in [n]}$ , which is chosen beforehand. Therefore, we have:

$$\mathsf{adv}_{11} = \mathsf{adv}_{12}.$$

This transition is the reverse of the transition from  $\mathbf{Hybrid}_6$  to  $\mathbf{Hybrid}_7$ . The secret keys are now generated using, for all  $t \in [\ell]$ :

$$\mathsf{sk}_t \leftarrow \widetilde{\mathsf{KeyGen}}_1 \left( \widetilde{\mathsf{msk}}, \left[ \mathbf{M}_t \right]_1, \left[ f^t(\boldsymbol{s}_t) \prod_{t < i \leq \ell} f^i(\boldsymbol{u}_i + \boldsymbol{x}) f^{\ell+1}(\boldsymbol{v} \otimes \boldsymbol{b}), \right]_1 \right),$$

and

$$\mathsf{sk}_{\ell+1} \leftarrow \widetilde{\mathsf{KeyGen}}_1 \left( \widetilde{\mathsf{msk}}, [\boldsymbol{m}_{\ell+1}]_1, [v]_1 \right),$$

{56}------------------------------------------------

where

$$[v]_1 = \left[ f(\boldsymbol{x}, \boldsymbol{v}, \boldsymbol{w}) + f(\boldsymbol{x}, \boldsymbol{y}, \boldsymbol{z}) + \sum_{i \in [\ell]} \Big( \prod_{j < i} f^j(\boldsymbol{x}) \Big) f^i(\boldsymbol{s}_i) \Big( \prod_{j > i} f^j(\boldsymbol{u}_j + \boldsymbol{x}) \Big) f^{\ell+1}(\boldsymbol{v} \otimes \boldsymbol{b}) \right]_1.$$

In  $\mathbf{Hybrid}_{13}$ , the challenge ciphertext  $(\overline{\mathsf{ct}}, \{\mathsf{ct}_i, \mathsf{ct}_j'\}_{i,j \in [n]})$  is as follows.  $\overline{\mathsf{ct}} \leftarrow \widetilde{\mathsf{Enc}}\left(\widetilde{\mathsf{msk}}\right)$ . For all  $i, j \in [n]$ :  $\mathsf{ct}_i \leftarrow \widehat{\mathsf{Enc}}\left(\widehat{\mathsf{msk}}, \begin{bmatrix} 0 \\ \boldsymbol{v}_i \end{bmatrix}_1\right)$ ,  $\mathsf{ct}_j' \leftarrow \widehat{\mathsf{KeyGen}}\left(\widehat{\mathsf{msk}}, \begin{bmatrix} 0 \\ -\boldsymbol{w}_i \end{bmatrix}_2\right)$ ,  $\overline{\mathsf{ct}} \leftarrow \widetilde{\mathsf{Enc}}\left(\widetilde{\mathsf{msk}}\right)$ .

This exactly corresponds to the experiment  $\mathsf{Ideal}_{\mathcal{A},\mathcal{S}}^{\mathsf{FE}}(1^{\lambda})$  for the simulator  $\mathcal{S} = (\widetilde{\mathsf{Setup}}, \widetilde{\mathsf{Enc}}, \widetilde{\mathsf{KeyGen}})$  defined in Fig.8.2.

Summing up, we have PPT adversaries  $\mathcal{B}_1$ ,  $\mathcal{B}_2$ ,  $\mathcal{B}_3$  and  $\mathcal{B}_4$  such that:

$$\mathsf{adv}^{\mathsf{SIM}}_{\mathsf{PHFE},\mathcal{A}}(\lambda) \leq \mathsf{adv}^{\mathsf{weak-SIM}}_{\widehat{\mathsf{IPFE}},\mathcal{B}_1}(\lambda) + (\ell+1) \cdot \mathsf{adv}^{\mathsf{DDH}}_{\mathbf{G}_2,\mathcal{B}_2}(\lambda) + 3 \cdot \mathsf{adv}^{\mathsf{DDH}}_{\mathbf{G}_1,\mathcal{B}_3}(\lambda) + \mathsf{adv}^{\mathsf{IND-FH}}_{\widehat{\mathsf{IPFE}},\mathcal{B}_4}(\lambda) + \frac{2}{p}.$$

$$\begin{split} &\underbrace{\widetilde{\operatorname{Petup}}(\operatorname{pp}):}_{\left(\widetilde{\operatorname{pk}},\,\operatorname{msk}\right)} \leftarrow \widetilde{\operatorname{Setup}}(\overline{\operatorname{pp}}). \; \operatorname{For \; all} \; i,j \in [n]: \; a_i,b_j \leftarrow_{\operatorname{R}} \; \operatorname{DDH}, \; v_i,w_j \leftarrow_{\operatorname{R}} \; \mathbb{Z}_p^2. \; \operatorname{For \; all} \; k \in [\ell]: \; u_k \leftarrow_{\operatorname{R}} \; \mathbb{Z}_p^n.}_{\operatorname{pk}:=\left(\widetilde{\operatorname{pk}}, \{[a_i]_1, [b_j]_2\}_{i,j \in [n]}\right), \; \widetilde{\operatorname{msk}}:=\left(\widetilde{\operatorname{msk}}, \{a_i,b_j,v_i,u_k\}_{i,j \in [n],k \in [\ell]}\right). \; \operatorname{Return \; pk}, \; \widetilde{\operatorname{msk}}.} \\ &\underbrace{\widetilde{\operatorname{Enc}(\operatorname{msk})}:}_{\left(\widetilde{\operatorname{pk}},\,\operatorname{msk}\right)} \leftarrow \widetilde{\operatorname{Setup}}(\widehat{\operatorname{pp}}), \; \overline{\operatorname{ct}} \; \leftarrow \; \widetilde{\operatorname{Enc}}\left(\widetilde{\operatorname{msk}}\right). \; \; \operatorname{For \; all} \; i,j \in [n]: \; \operatorname{ct}_i \; \leftarrow \; \widetilde{\operatorname{Enc}}\left(\widehat{\operatorname{msk}}, \begin{bmatrix} 0 \\ v_i \end{bmatrix}_1\right), \\ & \operatorname{ct}_j' \leftarrow \widetilde{\operatorname{KeyGen}}\left(\widehat{\operatorname{msk}}, \begin{bmatrix} 0 \\ -w_j \end{bmatrix}_2\right). \; \operatorname{Return}\left(\overline{\operatorname{ct}}, \{\operatorname{ct}_i, \operatorname{ct}_j'\}_{i,j \in [n]}\right).} \\ &\underbrace{\widetilde{\operatorname{KeyGen}}(\operatorname{msk}, (f^0, \dots, f^{\ell+1}), f(x,y,z), x):}_{\operatorname{For \; all} \; \; t \; \in \; [\ell], \; \; \operatorname{sk}_t \; \leftarrow \; \widetilde{\operatorname{KeyGen}}_1\left(\widehat{\operatorname{msk}}, [\mathbf{M}_\ell]_1, \left[f^\ell(s_\ell) \prod_{\ell < i \le \ell} f^i(u_i + x) f^{\ell+1}(v \otimes b), \right]_1}, \\ &\operatorname{sk}_{\ell+1} \leftarrow \widetilde{\operatorname{KeyGen}}_1\left(\widehat{\operatorname{msk}}, [m_{\ell+1}]_1, [v]_1\right), \; \operatorname{where} \\ &[v]_1 = \left[f(x,v,w) + f(x,y,z) + \sum_{i \in [\ell]} \left(\prod_{j < i} f^j(x)\right) f^i(s_i) \left(\prod_{j > i} f^j(u_j + x)\right) f^{\ell+1}(v \otimes b)\right]_1}. \\ &\operatorname{Return} \; \{\operatorname{sk}_\ell\}_{\ell \in [\ell+1]}. \end{aligned}$$

Figure 5: Simulator for the FE scheme depicted in Fig.8.2 for the functionality  $\mathcal{F}^{\mathsf{PHFE}}$ .

{57}------------------------------------------------

### <span id="page-57-0"></span>8.3 Constructing Inner-Product FE

Here, we build a public-key FE inner products, that is, the functionality  $\mathcal{F}^{\mathsf{IPFE'}} = \bigcup_{\dim \in \mathsf{poly}} \{\mathcal{F}_{\lambda,\dim(\lambda)}\}_{\lambda}$  defined with respect to the sequence of groups  $\mathcal{G} = \{\mathcal{G}_{\lambda}\}_{\lambda}$  where for all  $\dim \in \mathsf{poly}$ , all  $\lambda \in \mathbb{N}$ , the set of function  $\mathcal{F}_{\lambda,\dim(\lambda)}$  is the set of all functions  $f: \mathbf{G}_1^{\dim} \to \mathbf{G}_T$ , described by a vector  $[\boldsymbol{y}]_2 \in \mathbf{G}_2^{\dim}$ , such that given as input  $\boldsymbol{x} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{\dim}$ , it outputs  $[\boldsymbol{x}^{\mathsf{T}}\boldsymbol{y}]_T \in \mathbf{G}_T$ . Our scheme is presented in Fig.8.3.

It builds upon the inner-product FE from [ALS16], that relies on the DDH assumption in pairing-free cyclic groups. We instead use a pairing group  $\mathcal{G}_{\lambda} = (\mathbf{G}_1, \mathbf{G}_2, \mathbf{G}_T, p, P_1, P_2, e)$ , where the ciphertexts will consist of group elements in  $\mathbf{G}_1$ , and the ALS functional secret key are embedded in  $\mathbf{G}_2$ , instead of  $\mathbb{Z}_p$ . Decryption now yields the inner product in  $\mathbf{G}_T$ .

This simple modification of ALS scheme already satisfies a simulation-security where the simulator needs to know the values  $[x^\top y]_2 \in G_2$  and  $[y]_2 \in G_2^{\dim}$  in order to simulate the challenge ciphertext that encrypts  $[x]_1 \in G_1^{\dim}$  and the functional secret key associated to  $[y]_2 \in G_2^{\dim}$ . This security property is inherited from the ALS scheme, which was proven simulation-secure in [Wee17] (see also [AGRW17, Appendix A]). Note that this is weaker than the standard simulation security notion, given in Definition 4.3, where the simulator gets the output of the function, which in this case, is  $[x^\top y]_T \in G_T$ , not  $[x^\top y]_2$ .

For our purposes, we want it to be possible for the simulator to choose whether it simulates the adversary's view from the values  $[x^Ty]_2$ ,  $[y]_2$  or  $[x^Ty]_1$ ,  $[y]_1$ . We achieve this by giving two copies of the encryption, one in  $G_1$ , one  $G_2$ , and splitting each functional secret key in two additive secret shares summing up to the actual key, one in  $G_2$  and one in  $G_1$ . This simulation security relies on the fact that it is possible to produce both of these shares knowing the secret either in  $G_1$  or  $G_2$ .

Figure 6: This is IPFE, an FE scheme for the functionality  $\mathcal{F}^{\mathsf{IPFE'}}$ , with weak-simulation security.

{58}------------------------------------------------

### Linear efficiency.

Observe that the encryption time of any vector  $x \in \mathbb{Z}^{\dim}$  is proportional to  $\dim$ .

#### Correctness.

For any  $\boldsymbol{x},\boldsymbol{y}\in\mathbb{Z}^{\dim}$ :

$$[\bm{c}_1^{\top} \bm{k}_1 + \bm{c}_2^{\top} \bm{k}_2]_T = [\bm{c}^{\top} \bm{k}]_T = [\bm{x}^{\top} \bm{y}]_T.$$

<span id="page-58-0"></span>**Theorem 8.2** (Weak-simulation security). The scheme presented in Fig.8.3 is weakly-simulation secure (as per Definition 8.1) assuming the bilateral DLIN assumption. Namely, for any PPT adversary A, there exists a PPT adversary B such that:

$$\mathsf{adv}^{\mathsf{weak-SIM}}_{\mathsf{IPFE},\mathcal{A}}(\lambda) \leq \mathsf{adv}^{\mathsf{DLIN}}_{\mathcal{PG},\mathcal{B}}(\lambda) + \frac{1}{p}.$$

*Proof.* The proof proceeds using a series of hybrid games, described below. Let  $\mathcal{A}$  be a PPT adversary against the weak simulation security of the scheme. For any game  $\mathbf{Hybrid}_i$ , we denote by  $\mathsf{adv}_i := \Pr[1 \leftarrow \mathbf{Hybrid}_i(\mathcal{A})]$  the probability that  $\mathbf{Hybrid}_i$  returns 1 when interacting with  $\mathcal{A}$ .

- $\mathbf{Hybrid}_0$ : is the real experiment as given in Definition 8.1.
- $\underline{\mathbf{Hybrid}_1}$ : is the same as  $\mathbf{Hybrid}_0$ , except the challenge ciphertext is computed using  $\boldsymbol{c} = \begin{pmatrix} \boldsymbol{u} \\ \boldsymbol{x} + \mathbf{W} \boldsymbol{u} \end{pmatrix}$  with  $\boldsymbol{u} \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p^2$  instead of  $\boldsymbol{c} = \begin{pmatrix} \boldsymbol{a}r \\ \boldsymbol{x} + \mathbf{W} \boldsymbol{a}r \end{pmatrix}$  with  $r \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p$ , using the bilateral DLIN assumption. We show there exists a PPT adversary  $\mathcal{B}$  such that:

$$|\mathsf{adv}_0 - \mathsf{adv}_1| \leq \mathsf{adv}^{\mathsf{DLIN}}_{\mathcal{PG},\mathcal{B}}(\lambda).$$

The adversary  $\mathcal{B}$  takes as input a tuple  $([\mathbf{A}]_s, [\mathbf{z}]_s)_{s \in [1,2]}$ , where the vectors  $[\mathbf{z}]_s$  are of the form  $[\mathbf{A}\boldsymbol{r}]_s$  with  $\boldsymbol{r} \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p^2$  (case 1) or uniformly random over  $\mathbf{G}_s^2$  (case 2). The adversary  $\mathcal{B}$  samples  $\mathbf{W} \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p^{\dim \times 3}$ , upon which it can simulate the view of the adversary  $\mathcal{A}$  straightforwardly. In case 1, it simulate  $\mathbf{Hybrid}_0$ , whereas it simulates  $\mathbf{Hybrid}_1$  in case 2.

•  $\underline{\mathbf{Hybrid}_2}$ : is the same as  $\mathbf{Hybrid}_1$ , except the challenge ciphertext is computed using  $u \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p^3 \setminus \mathsf{Span}(\mathbf{A})$  instead of  $u \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p^3$ . This only induces a statistical change of 1/p since the size of  $\mathsf{Span}(\mathbf{A})$  is at most  $p^2$ . Thus:

$$|\mathsf{adv}_1 - \mathsf{adv}_2| \leq \frac{1}{p}.$$

• Hybrid<sub>3</sub>: is the same as Hybrid<sub>2</sub>, except the challenge ciphertext is computed using:

$$\boldsymbol{c} = \begin{pmatrix} \boldsymbol{u} \ \mathbf{W} \boldsymbol{u} \end{pmatrix},$$

{59}------------------------------------------------

where  $u \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p^3 \setminus \mathsf{Span}(\mathbf{A})$ . Besides, the functional keys are computed using:

$$\boldsymbol{k} = \begin{pmatrix} \boldsymbol{x}^\top \boldsymbol{y} - \mathbf{W}^\top \boldsymbol{y} \ \boldsymbol{y} \end{pmatrix}.$$

We show that these two games are identically distributed, using the fact that for any  $x \in \mathbb{Z}^{\dim}$  and  $a^{\perp} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^3$ , the following are identically distributed:

$$\mathbf{W}$$
 and  $\mathbf{W} - \boldsymbol{x}(\boldsymbol{a}^\perp)^\top,$ 

with  $\mathbf{W} \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p^{\dim \times 3}$ . We use that fact with  $\boldsymbol{x}$  the challenge chosen by the adversary, which is chosen beforehand, and therefore, independently of msk = W; and  $a^{\perp} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^3$  such that  $\mathbf{A}^{\top} \mathbf{a}^{\perp} = \mathbf{0}$  and  $\mathbf{u}^{\top} \mathbf{a}^{\perp} = 1$ . Note that such a vector exists since  $\mathbf{u} \notin \mathsf{Span}(\mathbf{A})$ . The leftmost distribution corresponds to  $\mathbf{Hybrid}_2$ , whereas the rightmost distribution corresponds to Hybrid<sub>3</sub>. Thus:

$$adv_2 = adv_3$$
.

It is clear hat  $\mathbf{Hybrid}_3$  corresponds to  $\mathsf{Ideal}_{\mathcal{A},\mathcal{S}}^{\mathsf{IPFE}}(1^{\lambda})$  with the simulator  $\mathcal{S}$  described in Fig.8.3. Consequently, we have:

$$\mathsf{adv}^{\mathsf{weak-SIM}}_{\mathsf{IPFE},\mathcal{A}}(\lambda) \leq \mathsf{adv}^{\mathsf{DLIN}}_{\mathcal{PG},\mathcal{B}}(\lambda) + \frac{1}{p}.$$

Setup(pp):

 $\overline{\mathbf{A}\leftarrow_{\mathtt{R}}\mathsf{DL}}\mathsf{IN},\,\mathbf{W}\leftarrow_{\mathtt{R}}\mathbb{Z}_p^{\dim \times 3},\,\boldsymbol{u}\leftarrow_{\mathtt{R}}\mathbb{Z}_p^3\setminus\mathsf{Span}(\mathbf{A}),\,\boldsymbol{a}^\perp\,\in\,\mathbb{Z}_p^3\,\,\mathrm{such\,\,that}\,\,\mathbf{A}^\top\boldsymbol{a}^\perp\,=\,\mathbf{0}\,\,\mathrm{and}\,\,\mathbb{R}_p^2$  $\boldsymbol{u}^{\top}\boldsymbol{a}^{\perp}=1$ . Return  $\widetilde{\mathsf{pk}}=\{[\mathbf{A}]_s,[\mathbf{WA}]_s\}_{s\in[1,2]}\}$  and  $\widetilde{\mathsf{msk}}=(\mathbf{W},\boldsymbol{u},\boldsymbol{a}^{\perp})$ .

Enc(msk):

$$\overline{\boldsymbol{c} = \boldsymbol{\big( \boldsymbol{u} \boldsymbol{u}} \boldsymbol{v}}$$
 . Return  $([\boldsymbol{c}]_1, [\boldsymbol{c}]_2)$  .

$$\frac{\text{For all } s \in [1,2], \, \widetilde{\text{KeyGen}}_s(\widetilde{\text{msk}}, [\boldsymbol{x}^\top \boldsymbol{y}]_s, [\boldsymbol{y}]_s):}{\text{Return} \begin{bmatrix} \boldsymbol{x}^\top \boldsymbol{y} \cdot \boldsymbol{a}^\perp - \mathbf{W}^\top \boldsymbol{y} \\ \boldsymbol{y} \end{bmatrix}_s}.$$

Figure 7: Simulator for the FE scheme from Fig.8.3 for the functionality  $\mathcal{F}^{\mathsf{IPFE'}}$ .

{60}------------------------------------------------

### <span id="page-60-0"></span>9 Construction of $\epsilon$ -Simulation Secure FE

In this section, we construct a  $\epsilon$ -simulation secure public-key functional encryption scheme FE for circuits  $\mathcal{F}^{\mathsf{CIRC}} = \{\mathcal{F}^{\mathsf{CIRC}}_{\lambda,\mathsf{prmtr}}\}_{\lambda,\mathsf{prmtr}}$  for some  $\epsilon \in (0,1)$ .  $\mathcal{F}^{\mathsf{CIRC}}_{\lambda,\mathsf{prmtr}}$  is the function class where for all  $\lambda$  and all polynomials  $\mathsf{prmtr} = (n,d,\ell,\mathsf{size})$  it denotes the set of Boolean circuits with input length  $n(\lambda)$ , depth at most  $d(\lambda)$ , output length  $\ell(\lambda)$ , and size at most  $\mathsf{size}(\lambda)$ . It uses the following ingredients:

- $\epsilon$ -1LGFE: a secret-key FE scheme for the function class  $\mathcal{F}^{CIRC}$  defined above, satisfying the following properties:
  - (Security.) 1-key single ciphertext  $\epsilon$ -simulation security as in Definition 4.4 for some constant  $\epsilon \in (0,1)$  specified later. Note that although the scheme is for a single key, it however allows circuits with multiple output bits.
  - (Efficiency.) *levelled* compactness as in Definition 4.6. In particular, ciphertext size as well as the size of encryption circuit is  $poly(\lambda, n, d)$ , independent of the function size size and output length  $\ell$ .
  - (Structural property.) Special Structure\* as per Definition 4.8. Recall, it says that:
    - \* (PP Syntax.) The pp generated by the PPGen( $1^{\lambda}$ , prmtr) algorithm contains a  $h_1(\lambda)$ -bit prime modulus which is the modulus of the bilinear map  $\mathcal{G}_{\lambda}$ , p.
    - \* (Linear secret key Structure.) The master secret key is a vector in  $s \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{h_2(\lambda)}$ . For any function  $f \in \mathcal{F}_{\lambda,\mathsf{prmtr}}$ , let  $f = \{f_i\}_{i \in [\ell]}$  denote the circuit computing  $i^{th}$  bit of f. The functional secret key is of the form  $\mathsf{sk}_f = \{\mathsf{sk}_{f_i}\}_{i \in [\ell]}$  where each  $\mathsf{sk}_{f_i} = \langle \mathsf{pp}_{f_i}, s \rangle + e_i \mod p$  where  $e_i \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \{0, \dots, h_3(\lambda, n, \ell, d)\}$  and  $\mathsf{pp}_{f_i}$  is some deterministic polynomial time computable function of  $\mathsf{pp}$  and  $f_i$ . For our construction below we require that  $h_3(\lambda, n, \ell, d) = 2^t 1$  for some natural number  $t = O(\log(n \cdot d \cdot \ell \cdot \mathsf{size}))$ . We can always choose an a constant  $\epsilon \in (0, 1)$  for the construction in Section 7 such that there exists an  $\epsilon$ -1LGFE scheme with this property, satisfying  $\epsilon$ -simulation security. We use that value of  $\epsilon$ .
    - \* (Linear + Round Decryption with polynomial decryption error.) There exists a deterministic poly-time algorithm such that given an encryption ct of  $m \in \{0,1\}^n$  and a function  $f = (f_1,\ldots,f_\ell) \in \mathcal{F}^{\mathsf{CIRC}}_{\lambda,\mathsf{prmtr}}$ , for every  $i \in [\ell]$ , computes  $\mathsf{ct}_{f_i}$  such that  $|\mathsf{ct}_{f_i} \langle \mathsf{pp}_{f_i}, \boldsymbol{s} \rangle f_i(m)\lceil \frac{p}{2}\rceil| \leq h_4(\lambda, d, \ell, \mathsf{size})$ . Given the secret-key for a function  $f = (f_1,\ldots,f_\ell)$ , this can be used to recover  $f(m) = (f_1(m),\ldots,f_\ell(m))$ .

Such a scheme is constructed in Section 7.

• PHFE: a public-key PHFE for the class of functions  $\mathcal{F}^{\mathsf{PHFE}}$  defined with respect to bilinear groups of order p (which is the same as the modulus of  $\epsilon$ -1LGFE) and is in fact the order of group  $\mathcal{G}_{\lambda}$ .  $\mathcal{F}^{\mathsf{PHFE}} = \{\mathcal{F}^{\mathsf{PHFE}}_{\lambda,n'}\}_{\lambda,n'}$  for every polynomial n' consists of all functions f that takes an input of the form  $(\boldsymbol{x},\boldsymbol{y}) \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{n'} \times \mathbb{Z}_p^{n'}$ , and computes  $f(\boldsymbol{x},\boldsymbol{y}) = [\sum_{j,k} f_{j,k}(\boldsymbol{x}) \cdot y_j \cdot y_k]_T \in \mathbf{G}_T$  where  $f_{j,k}$  is a constant degree polynomial over

{61}------------------------------------------------

x (i.e. an arithmetic circuit in NC<sup>0</sup>), and  $G_T$  denotes the target group (see pairing groups in Definition 3). The scheme PHFE satisfies the following properties:

- (Security.) 1-simulation security for unbounded key queries.
- (Efficiency.) Linear run-time as per Definition 4.6.

Such a scheme is constructed in Section 8 (in fact the scheme in Section 8 handles even a larger class of function, namely arithmetic  $NC^1$  on the public part of the input). We set n' later.

• sPRG: a structured-seed PRG with stretch  $\tau > 1$ , linear efficiency as per Definition 6.1. This sPRG works with the modulus  $p(\lambda)$  of the bilinear map  $\mathcal{G}_{\lambda}$ . The evaluation algorithm of sPRG computes an arithmetic NC<sup>0</sup> circuit on the public part of the seed, and a degree-2 polynomial on the secret part of the seed, that is, sPRG  $\in$  (arith-NC<sup>0</sup>, deg 2). This sPRG is implementable by  $\mathcal{F}^{\mathsf{PHFE}}$ .

We now describe the construction.

**Parameters:** For sPRG, we set the length parameter to be  $\ell^{\frac{1}{\tau}} \cdot \lambda$ . Thus,  $\ell_{\mathsf{sPRG}} = \ell^{\frac{1}{\tau}} \mathsf{poly}(\lambda)$  is the number of  $\mathbb{Z}_p$  elements in the sPRG seed for some polynomial poly independent of the  $\ell$ . Define  $n' = h_2(\lambda, d) + \ell_{\mathsf{sPRG}}$ . Let  $t = \log_2(h_3(\lambda, n, \ell, d) + 1)$ .

<span id="page-61-0"></span>**Construction:** Please refer to the construction in Figure 9.

**Correctness:** Consider a message  $m \in \{0,1\}^n$ , a circuit  $C \in \mathcal{F}_{prmtr}$ , an encryption of m, of the form  $\mathsf{ct} = (\mathsf{ct}_1, \mathsf{ct}_2)$ , and a functional decryption key  $\mathsf{sk}_C = (\mathsf{sk}_{C_1}, \dots, \mathsf{sk}_{C_\ell})$ . Recall that decryption computes the following:

- It computes  $[w_i]_T \leftarrow \mathsf{PHFE.Dec}(\mathsf{sk}_{C_i}, \mathsf{ct}_2)$ . By correctness of PHFE, we have  $[w_i]_T = [\langle \epsilon\text{-1LGFE.pp}_{C_i}, \mathsf{msk'} \rangle + \sum_{j \in [t]} 2^{j-1} \cdot r_{(i-1) \cdot t+j}]_T$ , where the bits  $r_{(i-1) \cdot t+j}$  denote the output of sPRG.
- By the special structure\* property of  $\epsilon$ -1LGFE, there exists a polynomial  $h_4$ , and a deterministic polynomial time algorithm that given  $\operatorname{ct}_1$  and  $C_i$ , outputs  $\operatorname{ct}_i$  such that  $\operatorname{ct}_i = \langle \epsilon$ -1LGFE. $\operatorname{pp}_{C_i}, \operatorname{msk}' \rangle + e_i + C_i(m) \cdot \lceil p/2 \rceil$ , where  $|e_i| \leq h_4(\lambda, n, d, \ell)$ .
- Then, the decryption computes  $[z_i]_T = [\operatorname{ct}_{C_i} w_i]_T$ . By the first observation above, we have  $w_i = \langle \epsilon\text{-1LGFE.pp}_{C_i}, \operatorname{msk}' \rangle + e_i'$ , where  $|e_i'| \leq h_3(\lambda, n, d, \ell)$ . Thus, if  $C_i(m) = 0$ ,  $|z_i| \leq h_3(\lambda, n, d, \ell) + h_4(\lambda, n, d, \ell)$ , otherwise it is large in absolute value. Thus the decryption suceeds.

**Sublinearity:** We now bound the size of the ciphertext and the size of the encryption circuit. We do that in two cases. This is our theorem:

**Theorem 9.1.** *If* PHFE satisfies linear efficiency and  $\epsilon$ -1LGFE satisfies (levelled) output sublinearity, and sPRG has a stretch of  $\tau > 1$ , then the FE scheme above satisfies ciphertext sublinearity. In addition, if sPRG satisfies linear efficiency then FE satisfies output sublinearity.

{62}------------------------------------------------

### $\mathsf{FE.PPGen}(1^{\lambda},\mathsf{prmtr}):$

Given  $1^{\lambda}$  and the tuple of polynomials prmtr =  $(n, \text{size}, d, \ell)$ , it samples PHFE.pp  $\leftarrow$  PHFE.PPGen $(1^{\lambda}, 1^{n'})$ ,  $\epsilon$ -1LGFE.pp  $\leftarrow$   $\epsilon$ -1LGFE.PPGen $(1^{\lambda}, \text{prmtr})$  and sPRG.pp  $\leftarrow$  sPRG.PPGen $(1^{\lambda}, 1^{\ell^{\frac{1}{\tau} \cdot \lambda}})$ ,  $I \leftarrow$  sPRG.IdSamp(sPRG.pp). Let p denote the prime modulus of  $\mathcal{G}_{\lambda}$ . Output pp = (PHFE.pp,  $\epsilon$ -1LGFE.pp, sPRG.pp, I, p).

 $\frac{\mathsf{FE}.\mathsf{Setup}(\mathsf{pp}) : \mathsf{Run} \ \mathsf{PHFE}.\mathsf{Setup}(\mathsf{PHFE}.\mathsf{pp}) \ \to \ (\mathsf{PHFE}.\mathsf{pk},\mathsf{PHFE}.\mathsf{msk}). \ \ \mathsf{Set} \ \ \mathsf{and} \ \ \mathsf{output}}{\mathsf{FE}.\mathsf{pk} = \mathsf{PHFE}.\mathsf{pk} \ \mathsf{and} \ \mathsf{FE}.\mathsf{msk} = \mathsf{PHFE}.\mathsf{msk}.}$ 

### $\mathsf{FE}.\mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{FE}.\mathsf{pk},m\in\{0,1\}^n):$

- $\mathsf{msk}' \leftarrow \epsilon\text{-1LGFE.Setup}(\epsilon\text{-1LGFE.pp})$
- $\mathsf{ct}_1 \leftarrow \epsilon\text{-1LGFE}.\mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{msk}', m).$
- $(P,S) \leftarrow SdSamp(I)$ .
- $ct_2 \leftarrow PHFE.Enc(PHFE.pk, (P, (S, msk'))).$

It returns  $ct = (ct_1, ct_2)$ .

FE.KeyGen(FE.msk, C): Given as input a circuit  $C \in \mathcal{F}_{prmtr}$ , denote  $C = (C_1, \ldots, C_\ell)$  where each  $C_i$  is the circuit computing the  $i^{th}$  output bit of C. For every  $i \in [\ell]$ , do the following:

- let  $\epsilon$ -1LGFE.pp $_{C_i}$  be the vector computed deterministically from  $\epsilon$ -1LGFE.pp and  $C_i$  such that  $\mathsf{sk}_{C_i} \approx \langle \mathsf{msk}', \epsilon$ -1LGFE.pp $_{C_i} \rangle$  (see the linear secret key structure in Definition 4.8).
- Compute  $\operatorname{sk}_{C_i} \leftarrow \operatorname{PHFE.KeyGen}(\operatorname{PHFE.msk}, f_i)$  where  $f_i$  takes as input  $(\mathsf{P}, (\mathsf{S}, \mathsf{msk'}))$  and outputs  $\langle \mathsf{msk'}, \epsilon\text{-1LGFE.pp}_{C_i} \rangle + \sum_{j \in [1,t]} 2^{j-1} \cdot r_{(i-1)\cdot t+j}$ , where for all  $\theta \in [m]$ ,  $r_{\theta}$  denotes the  $\theta$ 'th bit output by  $\operatorname{sPRG.Eval}(I,\operatorname{sd}) \in \{0,1\}^m$ .

It returns  $\mathsf{sk}_C = (\mathsf{sk}_{C_1}, \dots \mathsf{sk}_{C_\ell})$ .

FE.Dec( $\mathsf{sk}_C, \mathsf{ct}$ ): Parse  $\mathsf{sk}_C = (\mathsf{sk}_{C_1}, ..., \mathsf{sk}_{C_\ell})$  and  $\mathsf{ct} = (\mathsf{ct}_1, \mathsf{ct}_2)$ . For every  $i \in [\ell]$ , do the following:

- By the special structure\* of  $\epsilon$ -1LGFE, compute  $\mathsf{ct}_{C,i}$  using the ciphertext  $\mathsf{ct}_1$ .
- Compute  $[w_i]_T \leftarrow \mathsf{PHFE.Dec}(\mathsf{sk}_{C_i}, \mathsf{ct}_2)$ .
- Compute  $[z_i]_T = [\operatorname{ct}_{C_i} w_i]_T$ .
- Check if  $|z_i| \le h_3(\lambda, n, d, \ell) + h_4(\lambda, n, d, \ell)$  (by brute-force). If so set  $y_i = 0$ . Otherwise, set  $y_i = 1$ . Output  $(y_1, ..., y_\ell)$ .

Figure 8: Construction of Functional Encryption Scheme FE.

{63}------------------------------------------------

We first find out the size of an encryption. The ciphertext consists of two components:  $\mathsf{ct}_1$  and  $\mathsf{ct}_2$ . The size of  $\mathsf{ct}_1$  is  $\ell^{1-\gamma_1} \operatorname{poly}(\lambda, n, d)$  for some  $\gamma_1 > 0$  and some polynomial poly due to (levelled) output sublinear efficiency of  $\epsilon$ -1LGFE. The size of  $\mathsf{ct}_2$  is  $n' \cdot \operatorname{poly}(\lambda)$  for some polynomial poly due to the linear efficiency of PHFE. Note that  $n' = p_2(\lambda, d) + \ell^{\frac{1}{\tau}}\lambda$ . Thus, overall the size of the ciphertext is  $\ell^{\max\{1-\gamma_1,\frac{1}{\tau}\}} \cdot \operatorname{poly}(\lambda, n, d)$  for some polynomial poly.

Now, assume that sPRG in addition has linear efficiency. The size of the circuit computing ct is the size of the circuit computing ct<sub>1</sub> and ct<sub>2</sub>. ct<sub>1</sub> can be computed by a circuit of size  $\ell^{1-\gamma_1}$  poly $(\lambda,n,d)$  for some constant  $\gamma_1>0$  and some polynomial poly due to output sublinearity of  $\epsilon$ -1LGFE. The size of the circuit computing ct<sub>2</sub> is n' poly $(\lambda)$  due to linear efficiency of PHFE and sPRG. Thus, combined, the size of the circuit computing ct is also  $O(\ell^{\max\{1-\gamma_1,\frac{1}{\tau}\}})$  poly $(\lambda,n,d)$  for some polynomial poly.

**Security:** We now prove security. Let the parameters be set as described in the construction. Then, we prove the following:

<span id="page-63-0"></span>**Theorem 9.2.** *If*  $\epsilon$ -1LGFE *is* (*single-key*)  $\epsilon$ -*simulation secure,* PHFE *is many-key* 1-*simulation secure,* sPRG *is a secure structured seed PRG and parameters are set as described above, then* FE *is* (*single-key*)  $\epsilon$ -*simulation secure.* 

We now list hybrids. The first hybrid corresponds to the real security game, whereas the last hybrid is our ideal security game. This hybrid implicitly defines the simulated algorithms Setup, Enc and KeyGen. We argue indistinguishability between each one of them. The differences between hybrids are highlighted in red.

### $\mathbf{Hybrid}_0$ :

- Adversary outputs a circuit  $C \in \mathcal{F}_{\lambda,\mathsf{prmtr}}^{\mathsf{CIRC}}$  along with a message  $m \in \{0,1\}^n$ .
- Run PPGen as in the scheme to compute pp.
- Run PHFE.Setup(PHFE.pp)  $\rightarrow$  (PHFE.pk, PHFE.msk). Set FE.pk = PHFE.pk and FE.msk = PHFE.msk. Send FE.pk to the adversary.
- To encrypt the message m:
  - $\mathsf{msk}' \leftarrow \epsilon\text{-1LGFE.Setup}(\epsilon\text{-1LGFE.pp})$
  - $\mathsf{ct}_1 \leftarrow \epsilon\text{-1LGFE}.\mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{msk}', m).$
  - ${\bf -} \ ({\mathsf{P}},{\mathsf{S}}) \leftarrow {\mathsf{SdSamp}}(I).$
  - $\ \mathsf{ct}_2 \leftarrow \mathsf{PHFE}.\mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{PHFE}.\mathsf{pk}, (\mathsf{P}, (\mathsf{S}, \mathsf{msk'}))).$
  - Give  $(ct_1, ct_2)$  to the adversary.
- For generating the secret key for the circuit C, do the following steps. Denote  $C = (C_1, \ldots, C_\ell)$  where each  $C_i$  is the circuit computing the  $i^{th}$  output bit of C. For every  $i \in [\ell]$ , do the following:

{64}------------------------------------------------

- Let  $\epsilon$ -1LGFE.pp $_{C_i}$  be the vector computed deterministically from  $\epsilon$ -1LGFE.pp and  $C_i$  such that  $\mathsf{sk}_{C_i} \approx \langle \mathsf{msk}', \epsilon$ -1LGFE.pp $_{C_i} \rangle$  (see the linear secret key structure in Definition 4.8).
- Compute  $\operatorname{sk}_{C_i} \leftarrow \operatorname{PHFE.KeyGen}(\operatorname{PHFE.msk}, f_i)$  where  $f_i$  takes as input  $(\mathsf{P}, (\mathsf{S}, \mathsf{msk'}))$  and outputs  $\langle \mathsf{msk'}, \epsilon\text{-1LGFE.pp}_{C_i} \rangle + \sum_{j \in [1,t]} 2^{j-1} \cdot r_{(i-1)\cdot t+j}$ , where for all  $\theta \in [\ell]$ ,  $r_\theta$  denotes the  $\theta$ 'th bit output by  $\operatorname{sPRG.Eval}(I,\operatorname{sd}) \in \{0,1\}^\ell$ .
- Give to the adversary  $\mathsf{sk}_C = (\mathsf{sk}_{C_1}, \dots \mathsf{sk}_{C_\ell})$ .

In the next hybrid, we use the simulator for the PHFE scheme. These two hybrids are indistinguishable due to the security of the PHFE scheme.

### Hybrid<sub>1</sub>:

- Adversary outputs a circuit C along with a message m.
- Run PPGen as in the scheme to compute pp.
- Run PHFE. $\widetilde{\mathsf{Setup}}(\mathsf{PHFE.pp}) \to (\mathsf{PHFE.pk}, \mathsf{PHFE.td}).$  Set  $\mathsf{FE.pk} = \mathsf{PHFE.pk}$  and  $\mathsf{FE.msk} = \mathsf{PHFE.td}.$
- Compute  $msk' \leftarrow \epsilon$ -1LGFE.Setup( $\epsilon$ -1LGFE.pp)
- Compute  $(P, S) \leftarrow SdSamp(I)$ .
- Compute  $\operatorname{ct}_1 \leftarrow \epsilon$ -1LGFE.Enc( $\operatorname{msk}', m$ ).
- For  $i \in [\ell]$  let  $v_i = \langle \mathsf{msk}', \epsilon\text{-1LGFE.pp}_{C_i} \rangle + \sum_{j \in [1,t]} 2^{j-1} \cdot r_{(i-1)\cdot t+j}$  where  $r = \mathsf{sPRG.Eval}(I, \mathsf{sd})$ .
- Compute  $\operatorname{ct}_2 \leftarrow \mathsf{PHFE}.\widetilde{\mathsf{Enc}}(\mathsf{td},w)$  where  $w = (\mathsf{P}, \{f_i\}_{i \in [\ell]}, \{v_i\}_{i \in [\ell]}).$  Let  $\operatorname{\mathsf{ct}} = (\operatorname{\mathsf{ct}}_1, \operatorname{\mathsf{ct}}_2).$
- For all  $j \in [\ell]$ , compute  $\mathsf{sk}_{C_j} \leftarrow \mathsf{PHFE}.\widetilde{\mathsf{KeyGen}}(\mathsf{td}, f_j, w)$ . Let  $\mathsf{sk}_C = (\mathsf{sk}_{C_1}, \dots, \mathsf{sk}_{C_\ell})$ .
- Give to the adversary FE.pk,  $\mathsf{ct} = (\mathsf{ct}_1, \mathsf{ct}_2)$  and  $\mathsf{sk}_C = (\mathsf{sk}_{C_1}, \dots, \mathsf{sk}_{C_\ell})$ .

In the next, hybrid, we replace r with a random string. These two hybrids are indistinguishable due to the security of sPRG. For this to happen we need  $\ell \cdot t$  to be less than  $k^{\tau}$  where k is length parameter of sPRG. Note that the length parameter of sPRG is set as  $\ell^{\frac{1}{\tau}}\lambda$ , thus  $k^{\tau}=\lambda^{\tau}\cdot\ell$ . Since  $t=O(\log(\lambda\cdot n\cdot\ell\cdot d\cdot \text{size}))$ , this holds.

### $\mathbf{Hybrid}_2$ :

- Adversary outputs a circuit C along with a message m.
- Run PPGen as in the scheme to compute pp.
- Run PHFE. $\widetilde{\mathsf{Setup}}(\mathsf{PHFE.pp}) \to (\mathsf{PHFE.pk}, \mathsf{PHFE.td}).$  Set  $\mathsf{FE.pk} = \mathsf{PHFE.pk}$  and  $\mathsf{FE.msk} = \mathsf{PHFE.td}.$

{65}------------------------------------------------

- Compute msk<sup>0</sup> ← -1LGFE.Setup(-1LGFE.pp)
- Compute (P, S) ← SdSamp(I).
- Compute ct<sup>1</sup> ← -1LGFE.Enc(msk<sup>0</sup> , m).
- For i ∈ [`] let v<sup>i</sup> = hmsk<sup>0</sup> , -1LGFE.pp<sup>C</sup><sup>i</sup> i + P j∈[1,t] 2 j−1 · r(i−1)·t+<sup>j</sup> where r ←<sup>R</sup> {0, 1} `λ<sup>τ</sup> .
- Compute ct<sup>2</sup> <sup>←</sup> PHFE.Enc <sup>g</sup>(td, w) where <sup>w</sup> = (P, {fi}i∈[`] , {vi}i∈[`]). Let ct = (ct1, ct2).
- For all <sup>j</sup> <sup>∈</sup> [`], compute sk<sup>C</sup><sup>j</sup> <sup>←</sup> PHFE.KeyGen ^ (td, f<sup>j</sup> , w). Let sk<sup>C</sup> = (sk<sup>C</sup><sup>1</sup> , . . . ,sk<sup>C</sup>` ).
- Give to the adversary FE.pk, ct = (ct1, ct2) and sk<sup>C</sup> = (sk<sup>C</sup><sup>1</sup> , . . . ,sk<sup>C</sup>` ).

Finally in the last hybrid, we simulate ct1, v1, . . . , v` using -1LGFE.Sim. Note that this is possible since the values v1, . . . , v` exactly correspond to -1LGFE functional decryption keys (see the special structure\* property in Definition [4.8\)](#page-25-4) . These two hybrids are indistinguishable due to -simulation security of the -1LGFE scheme.

#### Hybrid<sup>3</sup> :

- Adversary outputs a circuit C along with a message m.
- Run PPGen as in the scheme to compute pp.
- Run PHFE.Setup ^(PHFE.pp) <sup>→</sup> (PHFE.pk, PHFE.td). Set FE.pk <sup>=</sup> PHFE.pk and FE.msk <sup>=</sup> PHFE.td.
- Compute td <sup>←</sup> -1LGFE.Setup ^(-1LGFE.pp)
- Compute (P, S) ← SdSamp(I).
- Let γ = -1LGFE.Sample(C, m).
- Compute ct<sup>1</sup> <sup>←</sup> -1LGFE.Enc <sup>g</sup>(td, γ) and for <sup>i</sup> <sup>∈</sup> [`], <sup>v</sup><sup>i</sup> <sup>←</sup> -1LGFE.KeyGen ^ (td, C<sup>i</sup> , γ).
- Compute ct<sup>2</sup> <sup>←</sup> PHFE.Enc <sup>g</sup>(td, w) where <sup>w</sup> = (P, {fi}i∈[`] , {vi}i∈[`]). Let ct = (ct1, ct2).
- For all <sup>j</sup> <sup>∈</sup> [`], compute sk<sup>C</sup><sup>j</sup> <sup>←</sup> PHFE.KeyGen ^ (td, f<sup>j</sup> , w). Let sk<sup>C</sup> = (sk<sup>C</sup><sup>1</sup> , . . . ,sk<sup>C</sup>` ).
- Give to the adversary FE.pk, ct = (ct1, ct2) and sk<sup>C</sup> = (sk<sup>C</sup><sup>1</sup> , . . . ,sk<sup>C</sup>` ).

In Hybrid<sup>3</sup> , only the value γ is needed to simulate the adversary's view, which is equal to C, C(m) with probability and equal to C, m with remaining probability. Namely, the simulator for FE does the following. Given as input the parameters pp, it samples FE.pk, -1LGFE.pk, using the simulator for PHFE and -1LGFE. Then it samples (P, S) ← SdSamp(I). It then simulates ct<sup>1</sup> and -1LGFE keys {vi}i∈[`] using the simulator for-1LGFE. This uses γ as an input. Then, it generates PHFE ciphertext ct<sup>2</sup> and keys {sk<sup>C</sup><sup>j</sup> }j∈[`] . This is done using simulator for PHFE scheme. This uses {vi}i∈[`] as an input, alongside P and function descriptions f<sup>j</sup> . This gives us the simulator for the scheme FE.

{66}------------------------------------------------

# <span id="page-66-0"></span>**10 Summing Up: Construction of** iO

We obtain the following main result:

**Theorem 10.1.** *Assuming the following assumptions hold:*

- *SXDH and bilateral DLIN assumptions over bilinear maps.*
- *Learning with Error assumption.*
- *A structured seed PRG* sPRG *exists (can be instantiated using a pseudorandom generator PRG,* G *satisfying* G − LWEleakD,,ρ *security for some constants* D ≥ 3 *and constants* , ρ ∈ (0, 0.5)*.)*

*There exists a (levelled) sublinearly efficient public-key functional encryption scheme for all polynomial sized circuits. Further, the scheme is subexponentially secure if each of these assumptions are subexponentially secure.*

Since subexponentially secure sublinearly-efficient public key encryption scheme implies iO [\[AJ15,](#page-68-6) [BV15,](#page-71-8) [KNT18\]](#page-73-10), we obtain the following result:

**Theorem 10.2.** *Assuming the subexponential versions of the following assumptions hold:*

- *SXDH and bilateral DLIN assumptions over bilinear maps.*
- *Learning with Error assumption.*
- *A structured seed PRG,* sPRG *exists (can be instantiated using a pseudorandom generator* G *satisfying* G−LWEleakD,,ρ *security for some constants* D ≥ 3 *and constants*, ρ ∈ (0, 0.5)*.)*

*Then, there exists an* iO *scheme for all circuits.*

We show the above by combining many theorems.

First, use Theorem [8.1](#page-49-0) to construct a PHFE scheme from SXDH and Bilateral DLIN assumptions. Then, use Theorem [6.1](#page-35-2) to construct a structured seed PRG from G−LWEleakD,,ρ assumption. Then, for any constant <sup>0</sup> ∈ (0, 1) use LWE to construct an 0 -simulation secure, (levelled) compact, single ciphertext, secret-key FE scheme satisfying special structure\* as described in Theorem [7.2.](#page-41-1)

Then, combine the above three ingredients to build a sublinearly efficient, public-key, single key <sup>00</sup>-simulation secure functional encryption scheme as described in Theorem [9.2](#page-63-0) for some constant <sup>00</sup> ∈ (0, 1).

Finally use Theorem [5.1,](#page-27-1) to construct a public-key sublinearly efficient FE with IND security. Observe that, if each of the assumptions are subexponentially secure, then the final result is also subexponentially secure.

We also observe that subsequent to the initial publication of our work, Wee [\[Wee20b\]](#page-75-6) showed how to construct a PHFE scheme from only the Bilateral DLIN assumption. By using Wee's theorem in place of Theorem [8.1,](#page-49-0) we obtain:

**Theorem 10.3.** *Assuming the following assumptions hold:*

{67}------------------------------------------------

- *Bilateral DLIN assumption over bilinear maps (immediately implied by the DLIN assumption over symmetric bilinear groups).*
- *Learning with Error assumption.*
- *A structured seed PRG* sPRG *exists (can be instantiated using a pseudorandom generator PRG,* G *satisfying* G − LWEleakD,,ρ *security for some constants* D ≥ 3 *and constants* , ρ ∈ (0, 0.5)*.)*

*There exists a (levelled) sublinearly efficient public-key functional encryption scheme for all polynomial sized circuits. Further, the scheme is subexponentially secure if each of these assumptions are subexponentially secure.*

and

**Theorem 10.4.** *Assuming the subexponential versions of the following assumptions hold:*

- *Bilateral DLIN assumption over bilinear maps (immediately implied by the DLIN assumption over symmetric bilinear groups).*
- *Learning with Error assumption.*
- *A structured seed PRG,* sPRG *exists (can be instantiated using a pseudorandom generator* G *satisfying* G−LWEleakD,,ρ *security for some constants* D ≥ 3 *and constants*, ρ ∈ (0, 0.5)*.)*

*Then, there exists an* iO *scheme for all circuits.*
